<p>The <b>Kingdom of Greece</b> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">Βασίλειον τῆς Ἑλλάδος</span>, <i>Vasíleion tīs Elládos</i>) was a state established in 1832 at the <a href="/wiki/London_Conference_of_1832" title="London Conference of 1832">Convention of London</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Powers" title="Great Powers">Great Powers</a> (the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/July_Monarchy" title="July Monarchy">France</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a>). It was internationally recognized by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Constantinople_(1832)" title="Treaty of Constantinople (1832)">Treaty of Constantinople</a>, where it also secured full <a href="/wiki/Independence" title="Independence">independence</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. This event also marked the birth of the first, fully independent, Greek state since the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> to the Ottomans in the mid-15th century.</p>
<p>The Kingdom succeeded from the <a href="/wiki/First_Hellenic_Republic" title="First Hellenic Republic">Greek provisional governments</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">Greek War of Independence</a>, and lasted until 1924. In 1924 the monarchy was abolished, and the <a href="/wiki/Second_Hellenic_Republic" title="Second Hellenic Republic">Second Hellenic Republic</a> was established. The restored Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> lasted from 1935 to 1973. The Kingdom was again dissolved in the aftermath of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_military_junta_of_1967-1974" title="Greek military junta of 1967-1974">seven-year military dictatorship</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Third_Hellenic_Republic" title="Third Hellenic Republic">Third Republic</a>, the current Greek government, came to be.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Frankokratia" title="Frankokratia">Frankokratia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Greece" title="Ottoman Greece">Ottoman Greece</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">Greek War of Independence</a></div>
<p>Most of <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> gradually became part of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> in the 15th century. The Eastern Roman or <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, the direct continuation to the ancient <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> who ruled most of the Greek-speaking world for over 1100 years, had been fatally weakened since the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Crusade" title="Fourth Crusade">sacking of Constantinople</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Latin Crusaders</a> in 1204.</p>
<p>The Ottoman advance into Greece was preceded by victory over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serb" title="Serb">Serbs</a> to its north. First the Ottomans won at 1371 on the <a href="/wiki/Maritsa" title="Maritsa">Maritsa</a> River — where the Serb forces were led by the King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vukasin_Mrnjavcevic" title="Vukasin Mrnjavcevic">Vukasin Mrnjavcevic</a>, the father of <a href="/wiki/Prince_Marko" title="Prince Marko">Prince Marko</a> and the co-ruler of the last emperor from the Serbian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nemanjic" title="Nemanjic">Nemanjic</a> dynasty. This was followed by a draw in the 1389 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kosovo" title="Battle of Kosovo">Battle of Kosovo</a>.</p>
<p>With no further threat by the Serbs and the subsequent Byzantine civil wars, the Ottomans <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">captured Constantinople</a> in 1453 and advanced southwards into Greece, capturing <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> in 1458. The Greeks held out in the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a> until 1460, and the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Venetians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Genoese</a> clung to some of the islands, but by 1500 most of the plains and islands of Greece were in Ottoman hands. The mountains of Greece were largely untouched, and were a refuge for Greeks to flee foreign rule and engage in guerrilla warfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> fell in 1571, and the Venetians retained <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> until 1670. The <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Islands" title="Ionian Islands">Ionian Islands</a> were only briefly ruled by the Ottomans (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kefalonia" title="Kefalonia">Kefalonia</a> from 1479 to 1481 and from 1485 to 1500), and remained primarily under the rule of Venice.</p>
<p>In 1821, the Greeks <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">rose up</a> against the Ottoman Empire. Following a protracted struggle, the autonomy of Greece was <a href="/wiki/London_Protocol_(1828)" title="London Protocol (1828)">first recognized</a> by the Great Powers (<a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bourbon_Restoration" title="Bourbon Restoration">France</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a>) in 1828; full independence was <a href="/wiki/London_Protocol_(1830)" title="London Protocol (1830)">recognized</a> in 1830. Count <a href="/wiki/Ioannis_Kapodistrias" title="Ioannis Kapodistrias">Ioannis Kapodistrias</a> became <a href="/wiki/List_of_heads_of_state_of_Greece" title="List of heads of state of Greece">Governor of Greece</a> in 1827, but was assassinated in 1831. At the insistence of the Powers, the 1832 <a href="/wiki/London_Conference_of_1832" title="London Conference of 1832">Treaty of London</a> made Greece a monarchy. <a href="/wiki/Pedro_I_of_Brazil" title="Pedro I of Brazil">Pedro of Braganza, Prince Royal of Portugal, Brazil, and the Algarves</a> was initially the first candidate for the Greek throne; however, he turned down the offer.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Otto_of_Greece" title="Otto of Greece">Otto of Wittelsbach, Prince of Bavaria</a> was chosen as its first <a href="/wiki/List_of_kings_of_Greece" title="List of kings of Greece">King</a>. Otto arrived at the provisional capital, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nafplion" title="Nafplion">Nafplion</a>, in 1833 aboard <a href="/wiki/HMS_Madagascar_(1822)" title="HMS Madagascar (1822)">a British warship</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reign_of_King_Otto_.281832.E2.80.931862.29">Reign of King Otto (1832–1862)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Reign of King Otto (1832–1862)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Otto_of_Greece" title="Otto of Greece">Otto of Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/3_September_1843_Revolution" title="3 September 1843 Revolution">3 September 1843 Revolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Otto_of_Greece_II.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="447" data-file-width="304" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Otto_of_Greece_II.jpg/200px-Otto_of_Greece_II.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Otto_of_Greece_II.jpg/300px-Otto_of_Greece_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d1/Otto_of_Greece_II.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Otto_of_Greece_II.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Otto_of_Greece" title="Otto of Greece">Otto</a>, the first King of modern Greece, in traditional Greek dress.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Otto's reign would prove troubled, but managed to last for 30 years before he and his wife, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Amalia_of_Greece" title="Queen Amalia of Greece">Queen Amalia</a>, left the way they came, aboard a British warship. During the early years of his reign a group of <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavarian</a> Regents ruled in his name, and made themselves very unpopular by trying to impose German ideas of rigid hierarchical government on the Greeks, while keeping most significant state offices away from them. Nevertheless, they laid the foundations of a Greek administration, army, justice system and education system. Otto was sincere in his desire to give Greece good government, but he suffered from two great handicaps, his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> faith, and the fact that his marriage to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Amalia_of_Greece" title="Queen Amalia of Greece">Queen Amalia</a> remained childless. In addition, the new Kingdom tried to eliminate the traditional <a href="/wiki/Banditry" title="Banditry">banditry</a>, something that in many cases meant conflict with some old revolutionary fighters (<a href="/wiki/Klepht" title="Klepht">klephtes</a>) who continued to exercise this practice.</p>
<p>The Bavarian Regents ruled until 1837, when at the insistence of <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, they were recalled and Otto thereafter appointed Greek ministers, although Bavarian officials still ran most of the administration and the army. But Greece still had no legislature and no constitution. Greek discontent grew until a <a href="/wiki/3_September_1843_Revolution" title="3 September 1843 Revolution">revolt</a> broke out in <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> in September 1843. Otto agreed to grant a constitution, and convened a National Assembly which met in November. The <a href="/wiki/Greek_Constitution_of_1844" title="Greek Constitution of 1844">new constitution</a> created a <a href="/wiki/Bicameralism" title="Bicameralism">bicameral parliament</a>, consisting of an Assembly (<i>Vouli</i>) and a Senate (<i>Gerousia</i>). Power then passed into the hands of a group of politicians, most of whom had been commanders in the War of Independence against the Ottomans.</p>
<p>Greek politics in the 19th century was dominated by the national question. Greeks dreamed of liberating them all and reconstituting a state embracing all the Greek lands, with <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> as its capital. This was called the Great Idea (<i><a href="/wiki/Megali_Idea" title="Megali Idea">Megali Idea</a></i>), and it was sustained by almost continuous rebellions against Ottoman rule in Greek-speaking territories, particularly <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(region)" title="Macedonia (region)">Macedonia</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> the British occupied <a href="/wiki/Piraeus" title="Piraeus">Piraeus</a> to prevent Greece declaring war on the Ottomans as a Russian ally.</p>
<p>A new generation of Greek politicians was growing increasingly intolerant of King Otto's continuing interference in government. In 1862, the King dismissed his Prime Minister, the former admiral <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Konstantinos_Kanaris" title="Konstantinos Kanaris">Constantine Kanaris</a>, the most prominent politician of the period. This provoked a military rebellion, forcing Otto to accept the inevitable and leave the country. The Greeks then asked Britain to send <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victoria_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Victoria of the United Kingdom">Queen Victoria</a>'s son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Duke_Alfred_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" title="Duke Alfred of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">Prince Alfred</a> as their new king, but this was vetoed by the other Powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Instead a young Danish prince became <a href="/wiki/George_I_of_Greece" title="George I of Greece">King George I</a>. George was a very popular choice as a constitutional monarch, and he agreed that his sons would be raised in the Greek Orthodox faith. As a reward to the Greeks for adopting a pro-British King, Britain ceded the <a href="/wiki/United_States_of_the_Ionian_Islands" title="United States of the Ionian Islands">United States of the Ionian Islands</a> to Greece.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_life">Religious life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Religious life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Greece" title="Church of Greece">Church of Greece</a></div>
<p>Under Ottoman rule, the Greek Church was a part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople</a>. The Muslims had no control over the church. With the establishment of the Greek kingdom, however, the government decided to take control of the church, breaking away from the patriarch in Constantinople. The government declared the church to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autocephalous" title="Autocephalous">autocephalous</a> (Independent) in 1833 in a political decision of the Bavarian Regents acting for <a href="/wiki/Otto_of_Greece" title="Otto of Greece">King Otto</a>, who was a minor. The decision roiled Greek politics for decades as royal authorities took increasing control. The new status was finally recognized as such by the Patriarchate in 1850, under compromise conditions with the issue of a special "Tomos" decree which brought it back to a normal status. As a result, it retains certain special links with the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mother_Church" title="Mother Church">Mother Church</a>". There were only four bishops, and they had political roles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1833 Parliament dissolved 400 small monasteries having fewer than five monks or nuns. Priests were not salaried; in rural areas he was a peasant farmer himself, dependent for his livelihood on his farm work and from fees and offerings by his parishioners. His ecclesiastical duties were limited to administering the sacraments, supervising funerals, the blessings of crops, and exorcism. Few attended seminaries. By the 1840s, there was a nationwide revival, run by traveling preachers. The government arrested several and tried to shut down the revival, but it proved too powerful when the revivalists denounced three bishops for purchasing their office. By the 1880s the "Anaplasis" ("Regeneration") Movement led to renewed spiritual energy and enlightenment. It fought against the rationalistic and materialistic ideas that had seeped in from secular Western Europe. It promoted catechism schools, and circles for the study the Bible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reign_of_King_George_I_.281863.E2.80.931913.29">Reign of King George I (1863–1913)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Reign of King George I (1863–1913)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/George_I_of_Greece" title="George I of Greece">George I of Greece</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:King_George_of_Hellenes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="907" data-file-width="669" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/King_George_of_Hellenes.jpg/200px-King_George_of_Hellenes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/King_George_of_Hellenes.jpg/300px-King_George_of_Hellenes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/King_George_of_Hellenes.jpg/400px-King_George_of_Hellenes.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:King_George_of_Hellenes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/George_I_of_Greece" title="George I of Greece">King George I of the Hellenes</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the urging of Britain and <a href="/wiki/George_I_of_Greece" title="George I of Greece">King George</a>, Greece adopted a much more democratic <a href="/wiki/Greek_Constitution_of_1864" title="Greek Constitution of 1864">constitution</a> in 1864. The powers of the King were reduced and the Senate was abolished, and the franchise was extended to all adult males. Nevertheless, Greek politics remained heavily dynastic, as it had always been. Family names such as Zaimis, Rallis and Trikoupis occurred repeatedly as Prime Ministers. Although parties were centered around the individual leaders, often bearing their names, two broad political tendencies existed: the liberals, led first by <a href="/wiki/Charilaos_Trikoupis" title="Charilaos Trikoupis">Charilaos Trikoupis</a> and later by <a href="/wiki/Eleftherios_Venizelos" title="Eleftherios Venizelos">Eleftherios Venizelos</a>, and the conservatives, led initially by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodoros_Deligiannis" title="Theodoros Deligiannis">Theodoros Deligiannis</a> and later by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thrasivoulos_Zaimis" title="Thrasivoulos Zaimis">Thrasivoulos Zaimis</a>.</p>
<p>Trikoupis and Deligiannis dominated Greek politics in the later 19th century, alternating in office. Trikoupis favoured co-operation with Great Britain in foreign affairs, the creation of infrastructure and an indigenous industry, raising protective tariffs and progressive social legislation, while the more populist Deligiannis depended on the promotion of Greek nationalism and the <i><a href="/wiki/Megali_Idea" title="Megali Idea">Megali Idea</a></i>.</p>
<p>Greece remained a very poor country throughout the 19th century. The country lacked raw materials, infrastructure and capital. Agriculture was mostly at the subsistence level, and the only important export commodities were <a href="/wiki/Ribes" title="Ribes">currants</a>, raisins and tobacco. Some Greeks grew rich as merchants and shipowners, and Piraeus became a major port, but little of this wealth found its way to the Greek peasantry. Greece remained hopelessly in debt to London finance houses.</p>
<p>By the 1890s Greece was virtually bankrupt, and public insolvency was declared in 1893. Poverty was rife in the rural areas and the islands, and was eased only by large-scale emigration to the United States. There was little education in the rural areas. Nevertheless, there was progress in building communications and infrastructure, and fine public buildings were erected in Athens. Despite the bad financial situation, Athens staged the <a href="/wiki/1896_Summer_Olympics" title="1896 Summer Olympics">revival of the Olympic Games</a> in 1896, which proved a great success.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oil_painting_of_the_Greek_Parliament,_at_the_end_of_the_19th_century,_by_N._Orlof.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="349" data-file-width="640" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Oil_painting_of_the_Greek_Parliament%2C_at_the_end_of_the_19th_century%2C_by_N._Orlof.jpg/220px-Oil_painting_of_the_Greek_Parliament%2C_at_the_end_of_the_19th_century%2C_by_N._Orlof.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Oil_painting_of_the_Greek_Parliament%2C_at_the_end_of_the_19th_century%2C_by_N._Orlof.jpg/330px-Oil_painting_of_the_Greek_Parliament%2C_at_the_end_of_the_19th_century%2C_by_N._Orlof.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Oil_painting_of_the_Greek_Parliament%2C_at_the_end_of_the_19th_century%2C_by_N._Orlof.jpg/440px-Oil_painting_of_the_Greek_Parliament%2C_at_the_end_of_the_19th_century%2C_by_N._Orlof.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

The <a href="/wiki/Hellenic_Parliament" title="Hellenic Parliament">Hellenic Parliament</a> in the 1880s, with PM <a href="/wiki/Charilaos_Trikoupis" title="Charilaos Trikoupis">Charilaos Trikoupis</a> standing at the podium.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Castaigne_-_Fencing_before_the_king_of_Greece,_1896_Summer_Olympics.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="736" data-file-width="864" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Castaigne_-_Fencing_before_the_king_of_Greece%2C_1896_Summer_Olympics.jpg/220px-Castaigne_-_Fencing_before_the_king_of_Greece%2C_1896_Summer_Olympics.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Castaigne_-_Fencing_before_the_king_of_Greece%2C_1896_Summer_Olympics.jpg/330px-Castaigne_-_Fencing_before_the_king_of_Greece%2C_1896_Summer_Olympics.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Castaigne_-_Fencing_before_the_king_of_Greece%2C_1896_Summer_Olympics.jpg/440px-Castaigne_-_Fencing_before_the_king_of_Greece%2C_1896_Summer_Olympics.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Castaigne_-_Fencing_before_the_king_of_Greece,_1896_Summer_Olympics.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fencing before King George, during the <a href="/wiki/1896_Summer_Olympics" title="1896 Summer Olympics">1896 Summer Olympics</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The parliamentary process developed greatly in Greece during the reign of George I. Initially, the royal prerogative in choosing his prime minister remained and contributed to governmental instability, until the introduction of the <i>dedilomeni</i> principle of <a href="/wiki/Confidence_and_supply" title="Confidence and supply">parliamentary confidence</a> in 1875 by the reformist <a href="/wiki/Charilaos_Trikoupis" title="Charilaos Trikoupis">Charilaos Trikoupis</a>. Clientelism and frequent electoral upheavals, however, remained the norm in Greek politics, and frustrated the country's development. Corruption and Trikoupis' increased spending to create necessary infrastructure like the <a href="/wiki/Corinth_Canal" title="Corinth Canal">Corinth Canal</a> overtaxed the weak Greek economy, forcing the declaration of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_bankruptcy" title="National bankruptcy">public insolvency</a> in 1893 and to accept the imposition of an International Financial Control authority to pay off the country's debtors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another political issue in 19th-century Greece was uniquely Greek: the language question. The Greek people spoke a form of Greek called <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">Demotic</a>. Many of the educated elite saw this as a peasant dialect and were determined to restore the glories of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a>. Government documents and newspapers were consequently published in <i><a href="/wiki/Katharevousa" title="Katharevousa">Katharevousa</a></i> (purified) Greek, a form which few ordinary Greeks could read. Liberals favoured recognising Demotic as the national language, but conservatives and the Orthodox Church resisted all such efforts, to the extent that, when the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> was translated into Demotic in 1901, riots erupted in Athens and the government fell (the <i>Evangeliaka</i>). This issue would continue to plague Greek politics until the 1970s.</p>
<p>All Greeks were united, however, in their determination to liberate the Greek-speaking provinces of the Ottoman Empire. Especially in <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Cretan_Revolt_(1866%E2%80%931869)" title="Cretan Revolt (1866–1869)">prolonged revolt in 1866–1869</a> had raised nationalist fervour. When war broke out between <a href="/wiki/Russo-Turkish_War_(1877%E2%80%931878)" title="Russo-Turkish War (1877–1878)">Russia and the Ottomans in 1877</a>, Greek popular sentiment rallied to Russia's side, but Greece was too poor, and too concerned of British intervention, to officially enter the war. Nevertheless, in 1881, <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a> and small parts of <a href="/wiki/Epirus_(region)" title="Epirus (region)">Epirus</a> were ceded to Greece as part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Berlin,_1878" title="Treaty of Berlin, 1878">Treaty of Berlin</a>, while frustrating Greek hopes of receiving <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crown_Prince_Palace_Athens_1909.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="406" data-file-width="700" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Crown_Prince_Palace_Athens_1909.jpg/220px-Crown_Prince_Palace_Athens_1909.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Crown_Prince_Palace_Athens_1909.jpg/330px-Crown_Prince_Palace_Athens_1909.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Crown_Prince_Palace_Athens_1909.jpg/440px-Crown_Prince_Palace_Athens_1909.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crown_Prince_Palace_Athens_1909.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Crown Prince's palace in 1909, today the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Mansion,_Athens" title="Presidential Mansion, Athens">Presidential Mansion</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Greeks in Crete continued to stage regular revolts, and in 1897, the Greek government under Theodoros Deligiannis, bowing to popular pressure, declared war on the Ottomans. In the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Greco-Turkish_War_(1897)" title="Greco-Turkish War (1897)">Greco-Turkish War of 1897</a> the badly trained and equipped Greek army was defeated by the Ottomans. Through the intervention of the Great Powers however, Greece lost only a little territory along the border to Turkey, while Crete was established as an <a href="/wiki/Cretan_State" title="Cretan State">autonomous state</a> under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prince_George_of_Greece" title="Prince George of Greece">Prince George of Greece</a>.</p>
<p>Nationalist sentiment among Greeks in the Ottoman Empire continued to grow, and by the 1890s there were constant disturbances in <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(region)" title="Macedonia (region)">Macedonia</a>. Here the Greeks were in competition not only with the Ottomans but also with the Bulgarians, engaged in an armed propaganda struggle for the hearts and minds of the ethnically mixed local population, the so-called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Struggle_for_Macedonia" title="Greek Struggle for Macedonia">Macedonian Struggle</a>". In July 1908, the <a href="/wiki/Young_Turk_Revolution" title="Young Turk Revolution">Young Turk Revolution</a> broke out in the Ottoman Empire.</p>
<p>Taking advantage of the Ottoman internal turmoil, <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a> annexed <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a> declared its independence from the Ottoman Empire. On Crete, the local population, led by a young politician named <a href="/wiki/Eleftherios_Venizelos" title="Eleftherios Venizelos">Eleftherios Venizelos</a>, declared <i><a href="/wiki/Enosis" title="Enosis">Enosis</a></i>, Union with Greece, provoking another crisis. The fact that the Greek government, led by <a href="/wiki/Dimitrios_Rallis" title="Dimitrios Rallis">Dimitrios Rallis</a>, proved unable to likewise take advantage of the situation and bring Crete into the fold, rankled with many Greeks, especially with young officers. These formed a secret society, the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_League" title="Military League">Military League</a>", with the purpose of emulating their Ottoman colleagues and seek reforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The resulting <a href="/wiki/Goudi_coup" title="Goudi coup">Goudi coup</a> on 15 August 1909 marked a watershed in modern Greek history: as the military conspirators were inexperienced in politics, they asked Venizelos, who had impeccable liberal credentials, to come to Greece as their political adviser. Venizelos quickly established himself as a powerful political figure, and his allies won the August 1910 elections. Venizelos became Prime Minister in October 1910, ushering a period of 25 years where his personality would dominate Greek politics.</p>
<p>Venizelos initiated a major reform program, including a <a href="/wiki/Greek_Constitution_of_1911" title="Greek Constitution of 1911">new and more liberal constitution</a> and reforms in the spheres of public administration, education and economy. French and British military missions were invited for the army and navy respectively, and arms purchases were made. In the meantime, the Ottoman Empire's weaknesses were revealed by the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Italo-Turkish_War" title="Italo-Turkish War">Italo-Turkish War</a> in Libya.</p>
<p>Through spring 1912, a series of bilateral agreements among the Balkan states (Greece, <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>) formed the <a href="/wiki/Balkan_League" title="Balkan League">Balkan League</a>, which in October 1912 declared war on the Ottoman Empire.</p>
