<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Balkan_Wars">The Balkan Wars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The Balkan Wars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Balkan_Wars" title="Balkan Wars">Balkan Wars</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Yugoslavian_front">Yugoslavian front</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Yugoslavian front">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gr%C3%A8ce_1ere_Guerre_balkanique.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1525" data-file-width="1754" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Gr%C3%A8ce_1ere_Guerre_balkanique.png/250px-Gr%C3%A8ce_1ere_Guerre_balkanique.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Gr%C3%A8ce_1ere_Guerre_balkanique.png/375px-Gr%C3%A8ce_1ere_Guerre_balkanique.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Gr%C3%A8ce_1ere_Guerre_balkanique.png/500px-Gr%C3%A8ce_1ere_Guerre_balkanique.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gr%C3%A8ce_1ere_Guerre_balkanique.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Greek operations during the First Balkan War (borders depicted are post-Second Balkan War)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Ottoman intelligence had disastrously misread Greek military intentions. In retrospect, it would appear that the Ottoman staffs believed that the Greek attack would be shared equally between the two major avenues of approach, Macedonia and Epirus. The 2nd Army staff had therefore evenly balanced the combat strength of the seven Ottoman divisions between the Yanya Corps and VIII Corps, in Epirus and Macedonia respectively. The Greek Army also fielded seven divisions, but, having the initiative, concentrated all seven against VIII Corps, leaving only a number of independent battalions of scarcely divisional strength in the Epirus front. This had fatal consequences for the Western Group of Armies, since it led to the early loss of the strategic center of all three Macedonian fronts, the city of <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Thessaloniki</a>, a fact that sealed their fate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> In an unexpectedly brilliant and rapid campaign, the Army of Thessaly seized the city. In the absence of secure sea lines of communications, the retention of the Thessaloniki-Constantinople corridor was essential to the overall strategic posture of the Ottoman Empire in the Balkans. Once this was gone, the defeat of the Ottoman Army became inevitable. To be sure, the Bulgarians and the Serbs played an important role in the defeat of the main Ottoman armies. Their great victories at Kirkkilise, Lüleburgaz, Kumanovo, and Monastir shattered the Eastern and Vardar Armies. However, these victories were not decisive in the sense that they ended the war. The Ottoman field armies survived, and in Thrace, they actually grew stronger day by day. In the strategic point of view these victories were enabled partially by the weakened condition of the Ottoman armies brought about by the active presence of the Greek army and fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek_infantry_1912.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="270" data-file-width="400" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d7/Greek_infantry_1912.jpg/250px-Greek_infantry_1912.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d7/Greek_infantry_1912.jpg/375px-Greek_infantry_1912.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d7/Greek_infantry_1912.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greek_infantry_1912.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Greek infantrymen in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sarantaporos" title="Battle of Sarantaporos">Battle of Sarantaporos</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With the declaration of war, the Greek Army of Thessaly under Crown Prince <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I_of_Greece" title="Constantine I of Greece">Constantine</a> advanced to the north, successfully <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sarantaporo" title="Battle of Sarantaporo">overcoming</a> Ottoman opposition in the fortified Straits of Sarantaporo. After another victory at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Yenidje" title="Battle of Yenidje">Giannitsa</a> on 2 November [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> 20 October] 1912, the Ottoman commander <a href="/wiki/Hasan_Tahsin_Pasha" title="Hasan Tahsin Pasha">Hasan Tahsin Pasha</a> surrendered Thessaloniki and its garrison of 26,000 men to the Greeks on 9 November [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> 27 October] 1912. Two Corps HQs (Ustruma and VIII), two Nizamiye divisions (14th and 22nd) and four Redif divisions (Salonika, Drama, Naslic and Serez) were thus lost to the Ottoman order of battle. Additionally, the Ottoman forces lost 70 artillery pieces, 30 machine guns and 70,000 rifles (Thessaloniki was the central arms depot for the Western Armies). The Ottoman forces estimated that 15,000 officers and men had been killed during the campaign in Macedonia, bringing total losses up to 41,000 soldiers. Another direct consequence was that the destruction of the Macedonian Army sealed the fate of the Ottoman Vardar Army, which was fighting the Serbs to the north. The fall of Thessaloniki left it strategically isolated, without logistical supply and depth to maneuver, ensuring its destruction.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Turks_prisoners.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="423" data-file-width="600" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4e/Turks_prisoners.jpg/250px-Turks_prisoners.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4e/Turks_prisoners.jpg/375px-Turks_prisoners.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4e/Turks_prisoners.jpg/500px-Turks_prisoners.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Turks_prisoners.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ottoman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prisoners_of_war" title="Prisoners of war">prisoners of war</a> in Greek hands. After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Giannitsa" title="Battle of Giannitsa">battle of Giannitsa</a>, the Greek Army took 26,000 Ottoman POWs.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Upon learning of the outcome of the battle of Yenidje, the Bulgarian high command urgently dispatched their 7th <i>Rila</i> Division from the north in the direction of the city. The division arrived there a week later, the day after its surrender to the Greeks. Until 10 November, the Greek-occupied zone had been expanded to the line from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Dojran" title="Lake Dojran">Lake Dojran</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pangaion_hills" title="Pangaion hills">Pangaion hills</a> west to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kavalla" title="Kavalla">Kavalla</a>. In southern Yugoslavia however, the lack of coordination between the Greek and Serbian HQs cost the Greeks a setback in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Vevi_(1912)" title="Battle of Vevi (1912)">Battle of Vevi</a> on 15 November [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> 2 November] 1912, when the Greek <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5th_Infantry_Division_(Greece)" title="5th Infantry Division (Greece)">5th Infantry Division</a> crossed its way with the VI Ottoman Corps (a part of the Vardar Army consisting of the 16th, 17th and 18th Nizamiye divisions), retreating to Albania following the battle of Prilep against the Serbs. The Greek division, surprised by the presence of the Ottoman Corps, isolated from the rest of Greek army and outnumbered by the now counterattacking Ottomans centered on <a href="/wiki/Bitola" title="Bitola">Bitola</a>, was forced to retreat. As a result, the Serbs beat the Greeks to Bitola.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Epirus_front">Epirus front</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Epirus front">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Epirus" title="Epirus">Epirus</a> front the Greek army was initially heavily outnumbered, but due to the passive attitude of the Ottomans succeeded in conquering <a href="/wiki/Preveza" title="Preveza">Preveza</a> (21 October 1912) and pushing north to the direction of <a href="/wiki/Ioannina" title="Ioannina">Ioannina</a>. On 5 November, Major <a href="/wiki/Spyros_Spyromilios" title="Spyros Spyromilios">Spyros Spyromilios</a> led a <a href="/wiki/Himara_revolt_of_1912" title="Himara revolt of 1912">revolt</a> in the coastal area of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Himar%C3%AB" title="Himarë">Himarë</a> and expelled the Ottoman garrison without facing significant resistance,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kondis_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kondis-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> while on 20 November Greek troops from western Macedonia entered <a href="/wiki/Kor%C3%A7%C3%AB" title="Korçë">Korçë</a>. However, Greek forces in the Epirote front had not the numbers to initiate an offensive against the German-designed defensive positions of <a href="/wiki/Bizani" title="Bizani">Bizani</a> that protected the city of Ioannina, and therefore had to wait for reinforcements from the Macedonian front.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the campaign in Macedonia was over, a large part of the Army was redeployed to Epirus, where Crown Prince Constantine himself assumed command. In the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bizani" title="Battle of Bizani">Battle of Bizani</a> the Ottoman positions were breached and Ioannina taken on 6 March [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> 22 February] 1913. During the siege, on 8 February 1913, the Russian pilot N. de Sackoff, flying for the Greeks, became the first pilot ever shot down in combat, when his biplane was hit by ground fire following a bomb run on the walls of Fort <a href="/wiki/Bizani" title="Bizani">Bizani</a>. He came down near small town of <a href="/wiki/Preveza" title="Preveza">Preveza</a>, on the coast north of the Ionian island of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lefkas" title="Lefkas">Lefkas</a>, secured local Greek assistance, repaired his plane and resumed flight back to base.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baker_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-12">[12]</a></sup> The fall of Ioannina allowed the Greek army to continue its advance into <a href="/wiki/Northern_Epirus" title="Northern Epirus">northern Epirus</a>, the southern part of modern Albania, which it occupied. There its advance stopped, although the Serbian line of control was very close to the north.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Naval_operations_in_the_Aegean_and_Ionian_seas">Naval operations in the Aegean and Ionian seas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Naval operations in the Aegean and Ionian seas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek_warships_1912-10-05.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="904" data-file-width="1200" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Greek_warships_1912-10-05.jpg/250px-Greek_warships_1912-10-05.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Greek_warships_1912-10-05.jpg/375px-Greek_warships_1912-10-05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Greek_warships_1912-10-05.jpg/500px-Greek_warships_1912-10-05.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greek_warships_1912-10-05.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Greek fleet assembled at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phaleron_Bay" title="Phaleron Bay">Phaleron Bay</a> on 5/18 October 1912, before sailing for Lemnos</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On the outbreak of hostilities on 18 October, the Greek fleet, placed under the newly promoted Rear Admiral <a href="/wiki/Pavlos_Kountouriotis" title="Pavlos Kountouriotis">Pavlos Kountouriotis</a>, sailed for the island of <a href="/wiki/Lemnos" title="Lemnos">Lemnos</a>, occupying it three days later (although fighting continued on the island until 27 October) and establishing an anchorage at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moudros_Bay" title="Moudros Bay">Moudros Bay</a>. This move was of major strategic importance, as it provided the Greeks with a forward base in close distance to the Dardanelles, the Ottoman fleet's main anchorage and refuge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall64_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hall64-14">[14]</a></sup> In view of the Ottoman fleet's superiority in speed and <a href="/wiki/Broadside" title="Broadside">broadside</a> weight, the Greek planners expected it to sortie from the straits early in the war. Given the Greek fleet's unpreparedness resulting from the premature outbreak of the war, such an early Ottoman attack might well have been able to achieve a crucial victory. Instead, the Ottoman Navy spent the first two months of the war in operations against the Bulgarians in the Black Sea, giving the Greeks valuable time to complete their preparations and allowing them to consolidate their control of the Aegean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>By mid-November Greek naval detachments had seized the islands of <a href="/wiki/Imbros" title="Imbros">Imbros</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thasos" title="Thasos">Thasos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agios_Efstratios" title="Agios Efstratios">Agios Efstratios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samothrace" title="Samothrace">Samothrace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psara" title="Psara">Psara</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ikaria" title="Ikaria">Ikaria</a>, while landings were undertaken on the larger islands of <a href="/wiki/Lesbos" title="Lesbos">Lesbos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chios" title="Chios">Chios</a> only on 21 and 27 November respectively. Substantial Ottoman garrisons were present on the latter, and their resistance was fierce. They withdrew into the mountainous interior and were not subdued until 22 December and 3 January respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall64_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hall64-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Samos" title="Samos">Samos</a>, officially an <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Samos" title="Principality of Samos">autonomous principality</a>, was not attacked until 13 March 1913, out of a desire not to upset the Italians in the nearby <a href="/wiki/Dodecanese" title="Dodecanese">Dodecanese</a>. The clashes there were short-lived as the Ottoman forces withdrew to the Anatolian mainland, so that the island was securely in Greek hands by 16 March.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall64_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hall64-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek_torpedo-boat_Nikopolis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="458" data-file-width="750" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Greek_torpedo-boat_Nikopolis.jpg/250px-Greek_torpedo-boat_Nikopolis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Greek_torpedo-boat_Nikopolis.jpg/375px-Greek_torpedo-boat_Nikopolis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Greek_torpedo-boat_Nikopolis.jpg/500px-Greek_torpedo-boat_Nikopolis.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greek_torpedo-boat_Nikopolis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The torpedo boat <i>Nikopolis</i>, former Ottoman <i>Antalya</i>, captured at Preveza by the Greeks</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the same time, with the aid of numerous merchant ships converted to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auxiliary_cruiser" title="Auxiliary cruiser">auxiliary cruisers</a>, a loose naval blockade on the Ottoman coasts from the Dardanelles to <a href="/wiki/Suez" title="Suez">Suez</a> was instituted, which disrupted the Ottomans' flow of supplies (only the Black Sea routes to <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> remained open) and left some 250,000 Ottoman troops immobilized in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Sea" title="Ionian Sea">Ionian Sea</a>, the Greek fleet operated without opposition, ferrying supplies for the army units in the Epirus front. Furthermore, the Greeks bombarded and then blockaded the port of <a href="/wiki/Vlor%C3%AB" title="Vlorë">Vlorë</a> in Albania on 3 December, and <a href="/wiki/Durr%C3%ABs" title="Durrës">Durrës</a> on 27 February. A naval blockade extending from the pre-war Greek border to Vlorë was also instituted on 3 December, isolating the newly established <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Government_of_Albania" title="Provisional Government of Albania">Provisional Government of Albania</a> based there from any outside support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lieutenant <a href="/wiki/Nikolaos_Votsis" title="Nikolaos Votsis">Nikolaos Votsis</a> scored a major success for Greek morale on 31 October: he sailed his <a href="/wiki/Torpedo_boat" title="Torpedo boat">torpedo boat</a> No. 11, under the cover of night, into the harbor of <a href="/wiki/Thessaloniki" title="Thessaloniki">Thessaloniki</a>, sank the old Ottoman ironclad battleship <i><a href="/wiki/Ottoman_ironclad_Feth-i_B%C3%BClend" title="Ottoman ironclad Feth-i Bülend">Feth-i Bülend</a></i> and escaped unharmed. On the same day, Greek troops of the Epirus Army seized the Ottoman naval base of <a href="/wiki/Preveza" title="Preveza">Preveza</a>. The Ottomans scuttled the four ships present there, but the Greeks were able to salvage the Italian-built torpedo-boats <i>Antalya</i> and <i>Tokat</i>, which were commissioned into the Greek Navy as <i>Nikopolis</i> and <i>Tatoi</i> respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> On 9 November, the wooden Ottoman armed steamer <i>Trabzon</i> was intercepted and sunk by the Greek torpedo boat No. 14 under Lt. <a href="/wiki/Periklis_Argyropoulos_(admiral)" title="Periklis Argyropoulos (admiral)">Periklis Argyropoulos</a> off <a href="/wiki/Ayval%C4%B1k" title="Ayvalık">Ayvalık</a>.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Confrontations_off_the_Dardanelles">Confrontations off the Dardanelles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Confrontations off the Dardanelles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_Elli.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="784" data-file-width="1300" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Battle_Elli.jpg/250px-Battle_Elli.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Battle_Elli.jpg/375px-Battle_Elli.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Battle_Elli.jpg/500px-Battle_Elli.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_Elli.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The Naval Battle of Elli</i>, oil painting by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vassileios_Chatzis" title="Vassileios Chatzis">Vassileios Chatzis</a>, 1913.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The main Ottoman fleet remained inside the <a href="/wiki/Dardanelles" title="Dardanelles">Dardanelles</a> for the early part of the war, while the Greek destroyers continuously patrolled the straits' exit to report on a possible sortie. Kountouriotis suggested <a href="/wiki/Naval_mine" title="Naval mine">mining</a> the straits, but was not taken up for fear of international reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fotakis50_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fotakis50-22">[22]</a></sup> On 7 December, the head of the Ottoman fleet Tahir Bey was replaced by Ramiz Naman Bey, the leader of the hawkish faction among the officer corps. A new strategy was agreed, whereby the Ottomans were to take advantage of any absence of <i>Averof</i> to attack the other Greek ships. The Ottoman staff formulated a plan to lure a number of the Greek destroyers on patrol into a trap. A first such effort on 12 December failed due to boiler trouble, but the second try two days later resulted in an indecisive engagement between the Greek destroyers and the cruiser <i>Mecidiye</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>The war's first major fleet action, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naval_Battle_of_Elli" title="Naval Battle of Elli">Naval Battle of Elli</a>, was fought two days later, on 16 December [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> 3 December] 1912. The Ottoman fleet, with four battleships, nine destroyers and six torpedo boats, sailed to the entrance of the straits. The lighter Ottoman vessels remained behind, but the battleship squadron moved on north under cover of the forts at <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kumkale&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kumkale (page does not exist)">Kumkale</a> and engaged the Greek fleet, coming from Imbros, at 9:40. Leaving the older battleships behind, Kountouriotis led the <i>Averof</i> into independent action: utilizing its superior speed, it cut across the Ottoman fleet's bow. Under fire from two sides, the Ottomans were quickly forced to withdraw to the Dardanelles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fotakis50_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fotakis50-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSN22_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-OSN22-24">[24]</a></sup> The whole engagement lasted less than an hour, in which the Ottoman suffered heavy damage to the <i>Barbaros Hayreddin</i> and 18 dead and 41 wounded (most during their disorderly retreat) and the Greeks one dead and seven wounded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fotakis50_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fotakis50-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the aftermath of Elli, on 20 December the energetic Lt. Commander <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rauf_Bey" title="Rauf Bey">Rauf Bey</a> was placed in effective command of the Ottoman fleet. Two days later he led his forces out, hoping again to trap the patrolling Greek destroyers between two divisions of the Ottoman fleet, one heading for Imbros and the other waiting at the entrance of the straits. The plan failed as the Greek ships quickly broke contact, while at the same time the <i>Mecidiye</i> came under attack by the Greek submarine <i>Delfin</i>, which launched a torpedo against it but missed; the first such attack in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSN22_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-OSN22-24">[24]</a></sup> During this time, the Ottoman Army continued to press upon a reluctant Navy a plan for the re-occupation of Tenedos, which the Greek destroyers used as a base, by an amphibious operation. The operation was scheduled for 4 January. On that day, weather conditions were ideal and the fleet was ready, but the <i>Yenihan</i> regiment earmarked for the operation failed to arrive on time. The naval staff nevertheless ordered the fleet to sortie, and an engagement developed with the Greek fleet, without any significant results on either side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Similar sorties followed on 10 and 11 January, but the results of these "cat and mouse" operations were always the same: "the Greek destroyers always managed to remain outside the Ottoman warships' range, and each time the cruisers fired a few rounds before breaking off the chase."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Savuranoglu_hamidiye.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="468" data-file-width="595" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Savuranoglu_hamidiye.jpg/250px-Savuranoglu_hamidiye.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Savuranoglu_hamidiye.jpg/375px-Savuranoglu_hamidiye.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Savuranoglu_hamidiye.jpg/500px-Savuranoglu_hamidiye.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Savuranoglu_hamidiye.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Ottoman cruiser <i><a href="/wiki/Ottoman_cruiser_Hamidiye" title="Ottoman cruiser Hamidiye">Hamidiye</a></i>. Its exploits during its eight-month cruise through the Mediterranean were a major morale booster for the Ottomans.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In preparation for the next attempt to break the Greek blockade, the Ottoman Admiralty decided to create a diversion by sending the light cruiser <i><a href="/wiki/Ottoman_cruiser_Hamidiye" title="Ottoman cruiser Hamidiye">Hamidiye</a></i>, captained by Rauf Bey, to raid Greek merchant shipping in the Aegean. It was hoped that the <i>Averof</i>, the only major Greek unit fast enough to catch the <i>Hamidiye</i>, would be drawn in pursuit and leave the remainder of the Greek fleet weakened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fotakis50_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-Fotakis50-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSN26_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-OSN26-28">[28]</a></sup> In the event, <i>Hamidiye</i> slipped through the Greek patrols on the night of 14–15 January and bombarded the harbor of the Greek island of <a href="/wiki/Syros" title="Syros">Syros</a>, sinking the Greek <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auxiliary_cruiser" title="Auxiliary cruiser">auxiliary cruiser</a> <i>Makedonia</i> which lay in anchor there (it was later raised and repaired). The <i>Hamidiye</i> then left the Aegean for the Eastern Mediterranean, making stops at <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a> and <a href="/wiki/Port_Said" title="Port Said">Port Said</a> before entering the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>. Although providing a major morale boost for the Ottomans, the operation failed to achieve its primary objective, as Kountouriotis refused to leave his post and pursue the <i>Hamidiye</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fotakis50_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-Fotakis50-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSN26_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-OSN26-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall65_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hall65-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Four days later, on 18 January [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> 5 January] 1913, when the Ottoman fleet again sallied from the straits towards Lemnos, it was defeated for a second time in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naval_Battle_of_Lemnos" title="Naval Battle of Lemnos">Naval Battle of Lemnos</a>. This time, the Ottoman warships concentrated their fire on the <i>Averof</i>, which again made use of its superior speed and tried to "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross_the_T" title="Cross the T">cross the T</a>" of the Ottoman fleet. <i>Barbaros Hayreddin</i> was again heavily damaged, and the Ottoman fleet was forced to return to the shelter of the Dardanelles and their forts. The Ottomans suffered 41 killed and 101 wounded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fotakis50_22-5"><a href="#cite_note-Fotakis50-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> It was the last attempt of the Ottoman Navy to leave the Dardanelles, thereby leaving the Greeks dominant in the Aegean. On 5 February [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> 24 January] 1913, a Greek <a href="/wiki/Farman_MF.7" title="Farman MF.7">Farman MF.7</a>, piloted by Lt. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Moutoussis" title="Michael Moutoussis">Moutousis</a> and with Ensign <a href="/wiki/Aristeidis_Moraitinis_(aviator)" title="Aristeidis Moraitinis (aviator)">Moraitinis</a> as an observer, carried out an aerial reconnaissance of the Ottoman fleet in its anchorage at Nagara, and launched four bombs on the anchored ships. Although it scored no hits, this operation is regarded as the first naval-air operation in military history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_Greece.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="472" data-file-width="622" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/New_Greece.jpg/200px-New_Greece.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/New_Greece.jpg/300px-New_Greece.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/New_Greece.jpg/400px-New_Greece.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_Greece.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Poster celebrating the "New Greece" after the <a href="/wiki/Balkan_Wars" title="Balkan Wars">Balkan Wars</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>General Ivanov, commander of the 2nd Bulgarian Army, acknowledged the role of the Greek fleet in the overall Balkan League victory by stating that "the activity of the entire Greek fleet and above all the <i>Averof</i> was the chief factor in the general success of the allies".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall65_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hall65-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_the_War">End of the War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: End of the War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_London_(1913)" title="Treaty of London (1913)">Treaty of London</a> ended the war, but no one was left satisfied, and soon, the four allies fell out over the partition of <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(region)" title="Macedonia (region)">Macedonia</a>. In June 1913, Bulgaria attacked Greece and Serbia, beginning the <a href="/wiki/Second_Balkan_War" title="Second Balkan War">Second Balkan War</a>, but was beaten back. The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Bucharest_(1913)" title="Treaty of Bucharest (1913)">Treaty of Bucharest</a>, which concluded the war, left Greece with southern Epirus, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Macedonia" title="Greek Macedonia">southern half</a> of Macedonia, Crete and the Aegean islands, except for the <a href="/wiki/Dodecanese" title="Dodecanese">Dodecanese</a>, which had been occupied by <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> in 1911. These gains nearly doubled Greece's area and population.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1914.E2.80.931924:_World_War_I.2C_crises.2C_and_first_abolition_of_Monarchy">1914–1924: World War I, crises, and first abolition of Monarchy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 1914–1924: World War I, crises, and first abolition of Monarchy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Greece_during_World_War_I" title="Greece during World War I">Greece during World War I</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Schism" title="National Schism">National Schism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_genocide" title="Greek genocide">Greek genocide</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor_Campaign" title="Asia Minor Campaign">Asia Minor Campaign</a>, and <a href="/wiki/11_September_1922_Revolution" title="11 September 1922 Revolution">11 September 1922 Revolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Constantineiofgreece.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1540" data-file-width="1000" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Constantineiofgreece.jpg/180px-Constantineiofgreece.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Constantineiofgreece.jpg/270px-Constantineiofgreece.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Constantineiofgreece.jpg/360px-Constantineiofgreece.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Constantineiofgreece.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
King Constantine I in German Field Marshal's uniform. His pro-German sympathies caused him to favour a course of neutrality in the First World War.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In March 1913, an anarchist, <a href="/wiki/Alexandros_Schinas" title="Alexandros Schinas">Alexandros Schinas</a>, assassinated King George in Thessaloniki, and his son came to the throne as Constantine I. Constantine was the first Greek king born in Greece and the first to be Greek Orthodox. His very name had been chosen in the spirit of romantic Greek nationalism (the <i><a href="/wiki/Megali_Idea" title="Megali Idea">Megali Idea</a></i>), evoking the Byzantine emperors of that name. In addition, as the Commander-in-chief of the Greek Army during the <a href="/wiki/Balkan_Wars" title="Balkan Wars">Balkan Wars</a>, his popularity was enormous, rivalled only by that of Venizelos, his Prime Minister.</p>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> broke out in 1914, despite Greece's treaty of alliance with Serbia, both leaders preferred to maintain a neutral stance. But when, in early 1915, the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allies</a> asked for Greek help in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dardanelles_campaign" title="Dardanelles campaign">Dardanelles campaign</a>, offering <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> in exchange, their diverging views became apparent: Constantine had been educated in <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Germany</a>, was married to <a href="/wiki/Sophia_of_Prussia" title="Sophia of Prussia">Sophia of Prussia</a>, sister of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II_of_Germany" title="Wilhelm II of Germany">Kaiser Wilhelm</a>, and was convinced of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a>' victory. Venizelos on the other hand was an ardent <a href="/wiki/Anglophile" title="Anglophile">anglophile</a>, and believed in an Allied victory.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venizelos_WWI_1918.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="359" data-file-width="550" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Venizelos_WWI_1918.jpg/250px-Venizelos_WWI_1918.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Venizelos_WWI_1918.jpg/375px-Venizelos_WWI_1918.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Venizelos_WWI_1918.jpg/500px-Venizelos_WWI_1918.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venizelos_WWI_1918.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Venizelos reviews a section of the Greek army on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedonian_front_(World_War_I)" title="Macedonian front (World War I)">Macedonian front</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First World War</a>, 1917. He is accompanied by Admiral <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pavlos_Koundouriotis" title="Pavlos Koundouriotis">Pavlos Koundouriotis</a> (left) and General <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Sarrail" title="Maurice Sarrail">Maurice Sarrail</a> (right).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since Greece, a maritime country, could not oppose the mighty British navy, and citing the need for a respite after two wars, King Constantine favored continued neutrality, while Venizelos actively sought Greek entry in the war on the Allied side. Venizelos resigned, but won the next <a href="/wiki/Greek_legislative_election,_May_1915" title="Greek legislative election, May 1915">elections</a>, and again formed the government. When <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a> entered the war as a German ally in October 1915, Venizelos invited <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Entente</a> forces into Greece (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedonian_front_(World_War_I)" title="Macedonian front (World War I)">Salonika Front</a>), for which he was again dismissed by Constantine.</p>
<p>In August 1916, after several incidents where both combatants encroached upon the still theoretically neutral Greek territory, Venizelist officers rose up in Allied-controlled Thessaloniki, and Venizelos established a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Movement_of_National_Defence" title="Movement of National Defence">separate government</a> there. Constantine was now ruling only in what was Greece before the Balkan Wars ("Old Greece"), and his government was subject to repeated humiliations from the Allies. In November 1916 the French occupied <a href="/wiki/Piraeus" title="Piraeus">Piraeus</a>, bombarded Athens and forced the Greek fleet to surrender. The royalist troops fired at them, leading to a battle between French and Greek royalist troops. There were also riots against supporters of Venizelos in Athens (the <i><a href="/wiki/Noemvriana" title="Noemvriana">Noemvriana</a></i>).</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hellenism_in_the_Near_East_1918.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1721" data-file-width="1403" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Hellenism_in_the_Near_East_1918.jpg/200px-Hellenism_in_the_Near_East_1918.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Hellenism_in_the_Near_East_1918.jpg/300px-Hellenism_in_the_Near_East_1918.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Hellenism_in_the_Near_East_1918.jpg/400px-Hellenism_in_the_Near_East_1918.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hellenism_in_the_Near_East_1918.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Kingdom" title="Greek Kingdom">Greek Kingdom</a> and the Greek diaspora in the Balkans and western Asia Minor, according to a 1919 map submitted to the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">Paris Peace Conference</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek_Flags_1921.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="296" data-file-width="441" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Greek_Flags_1921.jpg/200px-Greek_Flags_1921.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Greek_Flags_1921.jpg/300px-Greek_Flags_1921.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Greek_Flags_1921.jpg/400px-Greek_Flags_1921.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greek_Flags_1921.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Constantine_I_of_Greece" title="Constantine I of Greece">Constantine</a> decorating regimental war flags of the Greek Army during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Turkish_War_(1919%E2%80%931922)" title="Greco-Turkish War (1919–1922)">Greco-Turkish War (1919–1922)</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/February_Revolution" title="February Revolution">February Revolution</a> in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, however, the Tsar's support for his cousin was removed, and Constantine was forced to leave the country, without actually abdicating in June 1917. His second son <a href="/wiki/Alexander_of_Greece" title="Alexander of Greece">Alexander</a> became King, while the remaining royal family and the most prominent royalists followed into exile. Venizelos now led a superficially united Greece into the war on the Allied side, but underneath the surface, the division of Greek society into <a href="/wiki/Venizelism" title="Venizelism">Venizelists</a> and anti-Venizelists, the so-called <a href="/wiki/National_Schism" title="National Schism">National Schism</a>, became more entrenched.</p>
<p>With the end of the war in November 1918, the moribund Ottoman Empire was ready to be carved up amongst the victors, and Greece now expected the Allies to deliver on their promises. In no small measure through the diplomatic efforts of Venizelos, Greece secured <a href="/wiki/Western_Thrace" title="Western Thrace">Western Thrace</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Neuilly" title="Treaty of Neuilly">Treaty of Neuilly</a> in November 1919 and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Thrace" title="Eastern Thrace">Eastern Thrace</a> and a zone around <a href="/wiki/Smyrna" title="Smyrna">Smyrna</a> in western <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> (already under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occupation_of_%C4%B0zmir" title="Occupation of İzmir">Greek administration</a> since May 1919) in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_S%C3%A8vres" title="Treaty of Sèvres">Treaty of Sèvres</a> of August 1920. The future of Constantinople was left to be determined. But at the same time, a <a href="/wiki/Turkish_National_Movement" title="Turkish National Movement">nationalist movement</a> had arisen in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kemal_Atat%C3%BCrk" title="Kemal Atatürk">Mustafa Kemal</a> (later Kemal Atatürk), who set up a rival government in <a href="/wiki/Ankara" title="Ankara">Ankara</a> and was engaged in fighting the Greek army.</p>
<p>At this point, nevertheless, the fulfillment of the <i>Megali Idea</i> seemed near. Yet so deep was the rift in Greek society, that on his return to Greece, an assassination attempt was made on Venizelos by two royalist former officers. Even more surprisingly, Venizelos' <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(Greece)" title="Liberal Party (Greece)">Liberal Party</a> lost the <a href="/wiki/Greek_legislative_election,_1920" title="Greek legislative election, 1920">elections</a> called in November 1920, and in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_plebiscite,_1920" title="Greek plebiscite, 1920">referendum</a> shortly after, the Greek people voted for the return of King Constantine from exile, following the sudden death of Alexander. The United Opposition, which had campaigned on the slogan of an end to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor_Campaign" title="Asia Minor Campaign">war in Anatolia</a>, instead intensified it. But the royalist restoration had dire consequences: many veteran Venizelist officers were dismissed or left the army, while Italy and France found the return of the hated Constantine a useful pretext for switching their support to Kemal. Finally, in August 1922, the Turkish army shattered the Greek front, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_Smyrna" title="Great Fire of Smyrna">took Smyrna</a>.</p>
<p>The Greek army evacuated not only Anatolia, but also Eastern Thrace and the islands of <a href="/wiki/Imbros" title="Imbros">Imbros</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tenedos" title="Tenedos">Tenedos</a> (<a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lausanne" title="Treaty of Lausanne">Treaty of Lausanne</a>). A <a href="/wiki/Population_exchange_between_Greece_and_Turkey" title="Population exchange between Greece and Turkey">compulsory population exchange</a> was agreed between the two countries, with over 1.5 million Christians and almost half a million Muslims being uprooted. This catastrophe marked the end of the <i>Megali Idea</i>, and left Greece financially exhausted, demoralized, and having to house and feed a proportionately huge number of <a href="/wiki/Greek_refugees" title="Greek refugees">refugees</a>.</p>
<p>The catastrophe deepened the political crisis, with the returning army rising up under Venizelist officers and forcing King Constantine to abdicate again, in September 1922, in favour of his firstborn son, <a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Greece" title="George II of Greece">George II</a>. The "Revolutionary Committee", headed by Colonels <a href="/wiki/Stylianos_Gonatas" title="Stylianos Gonatas">Stylianos Gonatas</a> (soon to become Prime Minister) and <a href="/wiki/Nikolaos_Plastiras" title="Nikolaos Plastiras">Nikolaos Plastiras</a> engaged in a witch-hunt against the royalists, culminating in the "<a href="/wiki/Trial_of_the_Six" title="Trial of the Six">Trial of the Six</a>". In October 1923, <a href="/wiki/Greek_legislative_election,_1923" title="Greek legislative election, 1923">elections</a> were called for December, which would form a National Assembly with powers to draft a new constitution. Following a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leonardopoulos-Gargalidis_coup_attempt" title="Leonardopoulos-Gargalidis coup attempt">failed royalist coup</a>, the monarchist parties abstained, leading to a landslide for the Liberals and their allies. King George II was asked to leave the country, and on 25 March 1924, <a href="/wiki/Alexandros_Papanastasiou" title="Alexandros Papanastasiou">Alexandros Papanastasiou</a> proclaimed the <a href="/wiki/Second_Hellenic_Republic" title="Second Hellenic Republic">Second Hellenic Republic</a>, ratified by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_plebiscite,_1924" title="Greek plebiscite, 1924">plebiscite</a> a month later.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Restoration_of_Monarchy_and_the_4th_of_August_Regime">Restoration of Monarchy and the 4th of August Regime</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Restoration of Monarchy and the 4th of August Regime">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/4th_of_August_Regime" title="4th of August Regime">4th of August Regime</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Georgeiiofgreece.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2197" data-file-width="1593" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Georgeiiofgreece.jpg/180px-Georgeiiofgreece.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Georgeiiofgreece.jpg/270px-Georgeiiofgreece.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Georgeiiofgreece.jpg/360px-Georgeiiofgreece.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Georgeiiofgreece.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Greece" title="George II of Greece">George II of Greece</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Metaxas-regime-greek-fascism.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="445" data-file-width="703" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Metaxas-regime-greek-fascism.png/220px-Metaxas-regime-greek-fascism.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Metaxas-regime-greek-fascism.png/330px-Metaxas-regime-greek-fascism.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Metaxas-regime-greek-fascism.png/440px-Metaxas-regime-greek-fascism.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Metaxas-regime-greek-fascism.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The conservative regime of <a href="/wiki/Ioannis_Metaxas" title="Ioannis Metaxas">Ioannis Metaxas</a> (<a href="/wiki/4th_of_August_Regime" title="4th of August Regime">4th of August Regime</a>) adopted many of the ideas and symbolism of Italian Fascism. Here members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Organisation_of_Youth" title="National Organisation of Youth">National Organisation of Youth</a> give the <a href="/wiki/Roman_salute" title="Roman salute">Roman salute</a> to Metaxas.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 10 October 1935, a few months after he suppressed <a href="/wiki/1935_Greek_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt" title="1935 Greek coup d'état attempt">a Venizelist Coup</a> in March 1935, <a href="/wiki/Georgios_Kondylis" title="Georgios Kondylis">Georgios Kondylis</a>, the former Venizelist stalwart, abolished the Republic in another coup, and declared the monarchy restored. A rigged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_plebiscite,_1935" title="Greek plebiscite, 1935">plebiscite</a> confirmed the regime change (with an unsurprising 97.88% of votes), and King George II returned.</p>
<p>King George II immediately dismissed Kondylis and appointed Professor <a href="/wiki/Konstantinos_Demertzis" title="Konstantinos Demertzis">Konstantinos Demertzis</a> as interim Prime Minister. Venizelos meanwhile, in exile, urged an end to the conflict over the monarchy in view of the threat to Greece from the rise of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_(1861%E2%80%931946)" title="Kingdom of Italy (1861–1946)">Fascist Italy</a>. His successors as Liberal leader, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Themistoklis_Sophoulis" title="Themistoklis Sophoulis">Themistoklis Sophoulis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgios_Papandreou" title="Georgios Papandreou">Georgios Papandreou</a>, agreed, and the restoration of the monarchy was accepted. The <a href="/wiki/Greek_legislative_election,_1936" title="Greek legislative election, 1936">1936 elections</a> resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Greece" title="Communist Party of Greece">Communists</a> holding the balance. As no government could be formed, Demertzis continued on. At the same time, a series of deaths left the Greek political scene in disarray: Kondylis died in February, Venizelos in March, Demertzis in April and Tsaldaris in May. The road was now clear for Ioannis Metaxas, who had succeeded Demertzis as interim Prime Minister.</p>
<p>Metaxas, a retired royalist general, believed that an authoritarian government was necessary to prevent social conflict and, especially, quell the rising power of the Communists. On 4 August 1936, with the King's support, he suspended parliament and established the <a href="/wiki/4th_of_August_Regime" title="4th of August Regime">4th of August Regime</a>. The Communists were suppressed and the Liberal leaders went into internal exile. Patterning itself after <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a>'s Fascist Italy,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Metaxas' regime promoted various concepts such as the "Third Hellenic Civilization", the <a href="/wiki/Roman_salute" title="Roman salute">Roman salute</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Organisation_of_Youth" title="National Organisation of Youth">national youth organization</a>, and introduced measures to gain popular support, such as the Greek <a href="/wiki/Social_Insurance_Institute" title="Social Insurance Institute">Social Insurance Institute</a> (IKA), still the biggest social security institution in Greece.</p>
<p>Despite these efforts the regime lacked a broad popular base or a mass movement supporting it. The Greek people were generally apathetic, without actively opposing Metaxas. Metaxas also improved the country's defenses in preparation for the forthcoming European war, constructing, among other defensive measures, the "<a href="/wiki/Metaxas_Line" title="Metaxas Line">Metaxas Line</a>". Despite his aping of Fascism, and the strong economic ties with resurgent <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>, Metaxas followed a policy of neutrality, given Greece's traditionally strong ties to Britain, reinforced by King George II's personal anglophilia. In April 1939, the Italian threat suddenly loomed closer, as Italy <a href="/wiki/Italian_invasion_of_Albania" title="Italian invasion of Albania">annexed</a> <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>, whereupon Britain publicly guaranteed Greece's borders. Thus, when <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> broke out in September 1939, Greece remained neutral.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_Greece_during_World_War_II" title="Military history of Greece during World War II">Military history of Greece during World War II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greco-Italian_War" title="Greco-Italian War">Greco-Italian War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Greece" title="Battle of Greece">Battle of Greece</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axis_Occupation_of_Greece" title="Axis Occupation of Greece">Axis Occupation of Greece</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Greek_Resistance" title="Greek Resistance">Greek Resistance</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GambierParryMetaxasJorgeIIDalbiacYPapagosEnero1941--128421.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="259" data-file-width="450" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/GambierParryMetaxasJorgeIIDalbiacYPapagosEnero1941--128421.jpeg/200px-GambierParryMetaxasJorgeIIDalbiacYPapagosEnero1941--128421.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/GambierParryMetaxasJorgeIIDalbiacYPapagosEnero1941--128421.jpeg/300px-GambierParryMetaxasJorgeIIDalbiacYPapagosEnero1941--128421.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/GambierParryMetaxasJorgeIIDalbiacYPapagosEnero1941--128421.jpeg/400px-GambierParryMetaxasJorgeIIDalbiacYPapagosEnero1941--128421.jpeg 2x" width="200"/></a>
