<a href="/wiki/Ioannis_Metaxas" title="Ioannis Metaxas">Ioannis Metaxas</a> with <a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Greece" title="George II of Greece">King George II</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexandros_Papagos" title="Alexandros Papagos">Alexandros Papagos</a> during a meeting of the Anglo-Greek War Council.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-164-0389-23A,_Athen,_Hissen_der_Hakenkreuzflagge.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="795" data-file-width="559" height="284" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-164-0389-23A%2C_Athen%2C_Hissen_der_Hakenkreuzflagge.jpg/200px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-164-0389-23A%2C_Athen%2C_Hissen_der_Hakenkreuzflagge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-164-0389-23A%2C_Athen%2C_Hissen_der_Hakenkreuzflagge.jpg/300px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-164-0389-23A%2C_Athen%2C_Hissen_der_Hakenkreuzflagge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-164-0389-23A%2C_Athen%2C_Hissen_der_Hakenkreuzflagge.jpg/400px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-164-0389-23A%2C_Athen%2C_Hissen_der_Hakenkreuzflagge.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_101I-164-0389-23A,_Athen,_Hissen_der_Hakenkreuzflagge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The symbolic start of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axis_occupation_of_Greece_during_World_War_II" title="Axis occupation of Greece during World War II">Occupation</a>: German soldiers raising the <a href="/wiki/Reichskriegsflagge" title="Reichskriegsflagge">German War Flag</a> over the Acropolis. It would be taken down in one of the first acts of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Resistance" title="Greek Resistance">Greek Resistance</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Triple_Occupation_of_Greece.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="917" data-file-width="899" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Triple_Occupation_of_Greece.png/200px-Triple_Occupation_of_Greece.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Triple_Occupation_of_Greece.png/300px-Triple_Occupation_of_Greece.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Triple_Occupation_of_Greece.png/400px-Triple_Occupation_of_Greece.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Triple_Occupation_of_Greece.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The three occupation zones. Blue indicates the Italian, red the German and green the territory annexed by Bulgaria. The Italian zone was taken over by the Germans in September 1943.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Greek_Government_in_Exile_during_the_Second_World_War_CM2758.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="777" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/The_Greek_Government_in_Exile_during_the_Second_World_War_CM2758.jpg/200px-The_Greek_Government_in_Exile_during_the_Second_World_War_CM2758.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/The_Greek_Government_in_Exile_during_the_Second_World_War_CM2758.jpg/300px-The_Greek_Government_in_Exile_during_the_Second_World_War_CM2758.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/The_Greek_Government_in_Exile_during_the_Second_World_War_CM2758.jpg/400px-The_Greek_Government_in_Exile_during_the_Second_World_War_CM2758.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Greek_Government_in_Exile_during_the_Second_World_War_CM2758.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Greece" title="George II of Greece">George II</a> during his visit to a Greek fighter station, 1944.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite this declared neutrality, Greece became a target for Mussolini's expansionist policies. Provocations against Greece included the sinking of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_cruiser_Elli_(1912)" title="Greek cruiser Elli (1912)">light cruiser <i>Elli</i></a> on 15 August 1940. Italian troops crossed the border on 28 October 1940, beginning the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Italian_War" title="Greco-Italian War">Greco-Italian War</a>, but were stopped by determined Greek defence, and ultimately driven back into <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>. Metaxas died suddenly in January 1941. His death raised hopes of a liberalization of his regime and the restoration of parliamentary rule, but King George quashed these hopes when he retained the regime's machinery in place.</p>
<p>In the meantime, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> was reluctantly forced to divert German troops to rescue Mussolini from defeat, and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Greece" title="Battle of Greece">attacked Greece</a> through <a href="/wiki/Yugoslavia" title="Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a> and Bulgaria on 6 April 1941. Despite British assistance, by the end of May, the Germans had overrun most of the country. The King and the government escaped to Crete, where they stayed until the end of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Crete" title="Battle of Crete">Battle of Crete</a>. They then transferred to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, where a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_government_in_exile" title="Greek government in exile">government in exile</a> was established.</p>
<p>The occupied country was divided in three zones (German, Italian and Bulgarian) and in Athens, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puppet_regime" title="Puppet regime">puppet regime</a> was established. The members were either <a href="/wiki/Conservatism" title="Conservatism">conservatives</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nationalists" title="Nationalists">nationalists</a> with fascist leanings. The three <a href="/wiki/Quisling" title="Quisling">quisling</a> prime ministers were <a href="/wiki/Georgios_Tsolakoglou" title="Georgios Tsolakoglou">Georgios Tsolakoglou</a>, the general who had signed the armistice with the Wehrmacht, <a href="/wiki/Konstantinos_Logothetopoulos" title="Konstantinos Logothetopoulos">Konstantinos Logothetopoulos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ioannis_Rallis" title="Ioannis Rallis">Ioannis Rallis</a>, who took office when the German defeat was inevitable, and aimed primarily at combating the left-wing Resistance movement. To this end, he created the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collaborationist" title="Collaborationist">collaborationist</a> <a href="/wiki/Security_Battalions" title="Security Battalions">Security Battalions</a>.</p>
<p>Greece suffered terrible privations during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, as the Germans appropriated most of the country's agricultural production and prevented its fishing fleets from operating. As a result, and because a British blockade initially hindered foreign relief efforts, a <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Greece)" title="Great Famine (Greece)">wide-scale famine</a> resulted, when hundreds of thousands perished, especially in the winter of 1941-1942. In the mountains of the Greek mainland, in the meantime, several <a href="/wiki/Greek_Resistance" title="Greek Resistance">resistance movements</a> sprang up, and by mid-1943, the Axis forces controlled only the main towns and the connecting roads, while a "Free Greece" was set up in the mountains.</p>
<p>The largest resistance group, the <a href="/wiki/National_Liberation_Front_(Greece)" title="National Liberation Front (Greece)">National Liberation Front</a> (EAM), was controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Greece" title="Communist Party of Greece">Communists</a>, as was (Elas) led by Aris Velouchiotis and a civil war soon broke out between it and non-Communist groups such as the <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Greek_League" title="National Republican Greek League">National Republican Greek League</a> (EDES) in those areas liberated from the Germans. The exiled government in <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a> was only intermittently in touch with the resistance movement, and exercised virtually no influence in the occupied country.</p>
<p>Part of this was due to the unpopularity of the King George II in Greece itself, but despite efforts by Greek politicians, British support ensured his retention at the head of the Cairo government. As the German defeat drew nearer, the various Greek political factions convened in Lebanon in May 1944, under British auspices, and formed a government of national unity, under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Papandreou_(senior)" title="George Papandreou (senior)">George Papandreou</a>, in which EAM was represented by six ministers.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_Civil_War_.281946.E2.80.9349.29">Greek Civil War (1946–49)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Greek Civil War (1946–49)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_civil_war" title="Greek civil war">Greek civil war</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Papagos_alexandros.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="187" data-file-width="160" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Papagos_alexandros.jpg/140px-Papagos_alexandros.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ca/Papagos_alexandros.jpg 1.5x" width="140"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Papagos_alexandros.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexandros_Papagos" title="Alexandros Papagos">Alexandros Papagos</a>, commander of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_army" title="Greek army">Greek army</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Italian_War" title="Greco-Italian War">Greco-Italian War</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_civil_war" title="Greek civil war">Greek civil war</a>, later became also <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Greece" title="Prime Minister of Greece">Prime Minister</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>German forces withdrew on 12 October 1944, and the government in exile returned to Athens. After the German withdrawal, the EAM-ELAS guerrilla army effectively controlled most of Greece, but its leaders were reluctant to take control of the country, as they knew that <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Stalin</a> had <a href="/wiki/Percentages_agreement" title="Percentages agreement">agreed</a> that Greece would be in the British sphere of influence after the war. Tensions between the British-backed Papandreou and EAM, especially over the issue of disarmament of the various armed groups, leading to the resignation of the latter's ministers from the government.</p>
<p>A few days later, on 3 December 1944, a large-scale pro-EAM demonstration in Athens ended in violence and ushered an intense, house-to-house struggle with British and monarchist forces (the <i><a href="/wiki/Dekemvriana" title="Dekemvriana">Dekemvriana</a></i>). After three weeks, the Communists were defeated: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Varkiza_agreement" title="Varkiza agreement">Varkiza agreement</a> ended the conflict and disarmed ELAS, and an unstable coalition government was formed. The anti-EAM backlash grew into a full-scale "White Terror", which exacerbated tensions.</p>
<p>The Communists boycotted the <a href="/wiki/Greek_legislative_election,_1946" title="Greek legislative election, 1946">March 1946 elections</a>, and on the same day, fighting broke out again. By the end of 1946, the Communist <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Army_of_Greece" title="Democratic Army of Greece">Democratic Army of Greece</a> had been formed, pitted against the governmental National Army, which was backed first by Britain and after 1947 by the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.</p>
<p>Communist successes in 1947–1948 enabled them to move freely over much of mainland Greece, but with extensive reorganization, the deportation of rural populations and American material support, the National Army was slowly able to regain control over most of the countryside. In 1949, the insurgents suffered a major blow, as Yugoslavia closed its borders following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tito%E2%80%93Stalin_split" title="Tito–Stalin split">split</a> between Marshal <a href="/wiki/Josip_Broz_Tito" title="Josip Broz Tito">Josip Broz Tito</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>.</p>
<p>In August 1949, the National Army under Marshal <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Papagos" title="Alexander Papagos">Alexander Papagos</a> launched an offensive that forced the remaining insurgents to surrender or flee across the northern border into the territory of Greece's northern Communist neighbors. The civil war resulted in 100,000 killed and caused catastrophic economic disruption. In addition, at least 25,000 Greeks were either voluntarily or forcibly evacuated to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_bloc" title="Eastern bloc">Eastern bloc</a> countries, while 700,000 became displaced persons inside the country. Many more emigrated to <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and other countries.</p>
<p>The postwar settlement saw Greece's territorial expansion, which had begun in 1832, come to an end. The 1947 <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Treaties,_1947" title="Paris Peace Treaties, 1947">Treaty of Paris</a> required Italy to hand over the <a href="/wiki/Dodecanese" title="Dodecanese">Dodecanese</a> islands to Greece. These were the last majority-Greek-speaking areas to be united with the Greek state, apart from Cyprus which was a British possession until it became independent in 1960. Greece's ethnic homogeneity was increased by the postwar expulsion of 25,000 Albanians from Epirus (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chameria_Issue" title="Chameria Issue">Cham Albanians</a>).</p>
<p>The only significant remaining minorities are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_minority_(Greece)" title="Muslim minority (Greece)">Muslims in Western Thrace</a> (about 100,000) and a small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_language_(Greece)" title="Slavic language (Greece)">Slavic-speaking</a> minority in the north. Greek nationalists continued to claim southern <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a> (which they called <a href="/wiki/Northern_Epirus" title="Northern Epirus">Northern Epirus</a>), home of a significant Greek population (about 3%-12% in the whole of Albania<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>), and the Turkish-held islands of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imvros" title="Imvros">Imvros</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tenedos" title="Tenedos">Tenedos</a>, where there were smaller Greek minorities.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postwar_Greece_and_the_fall_of_monarchy_.281950.E2.80.931973.29">Postwar Greece and the fall of monarchy (1950–1973)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Postwar Greece and the fall of monarchy (1950–1973)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paul_and_Frederica_of_Greece.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="461" data-file-width="603" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Paul_and_Frederica_of_Greece.jpg/220px-Paul_and_Frederica_of_Greece.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Paul_and_Frederica_of_Greece.jpg/330px-Paul_and_Frederica_of_Greece.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Paul_and_Frederica_of_Greece.jpg/440px-Paul_and_Frederica_of_Greece.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paul_and_Frederica_of_Greece.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Greece" title="Paul of Greece">Paul of Greece</a> with his wife <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frederika_of_Greece" title="Frederika of Greece">Frederica</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Royal_Standard_of_the_Kingdom_of_Greece_(1936-1967).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="800" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Royal_Standard_of_the_Kingdom_of_Greece_%281936-1967%29.svg/160px-Royal_Standard_of_the_Kingdom_of_Greece_%281936-1967%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Royal_Standard_of_the_Kingdom_of_Greece_%281936-1967%29.svg/240px-Royal_Standard_of_the_Kingdom_of_Greece_%281936-1967%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Royal_Standard_of_the_Kingdom_of_Greece_%281936-1967%29.svg/320px-Royal_Standard_of_the_Kingdom_of_Greece_%281936-1967%29.svg.png 2x" width="160"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Royal_Standard_of_the_Kingdom_of_Greece_(1936-1967).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Royal Standard (1935-1973).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since the Civil war (1946–49) but even more after that, the parties in the parliament were divided in three political concentrations. The political formation Right-Centre-Left, given the exacerbation of political animosity that had preceded dividing the country in the 40s, tended to turn the concurrence of parties into ideological positions.</p>
<p>In the beginning of the 1950s, the forces of the Centre (EPEK) succeeded in gaining the power and under the leadership of the aged general N. Plastiras they governed for about half a four-year term. These were a series of governments having limited manoeuvre ability and inadequate influence in the political arena. This government, as well as those that followed, was constantly under the American auspices. The defeat of EPEK in the elections of 1952, apart from increasing the repressive measures that concerned the defeated of the Civil war, also marked the end of the general political position that it represented, namely political consensus and social reconciliation.</p>
<p>The Left, which had been ostracized from the political life of the country, found a way of expression through the constitution of EDA (United Democratic Left) in 1951, which turned out to be a significant pole, yet steadily excluded from the decision making centres. After the disbandment of the Centre as an autonomous political institution, EDA practically expanded its electoral influence to a significant part of the EAM-based Centre-Left.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Athens,_Greece_early_50%27s.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="548" data-file-width="781" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Athens%2C_Greece_early_50%27s.jpg/240px-Athens%2C_Greece_early_50%27s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Athens%2C_Greece_early_50%27s.jpg/360px-Athens%2C_Greece_early_50%27s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Athens%2C_Greece_early_50%27s.jpg/480px-Athens%2C_Greece_early_50%27s.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Athens,_Greece_early_50%27s.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Athens in the 1950s</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 1960s are part of the period 1953–72, during which Greek economy developed rapidly and was structured within the scope of European and worldwide economic developments. One of the main characteristics of that period was the major political event – as we have come to accept it – of the country's accession in the EEC, in an attempt to create a common market. The relevant treaty was contracted in 1962.</p>
<p>The developmental strategy adopted by the country was embodied in centrally organized five-year plans; yet their orientation was indistinct. The average annual emigration, which absorbed the excess workforce and contributed to extremely high growth rates, exceeded the annual natural increase in population. The influx of large amounts of foreign private capital was being facilitated and consumption was expanded. These, associated with the rise of tourism, the expansion of shipping activity and with the migrant remittances, had a positive effect on the balance of payments.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-71706-0032,_MS_%22V%C3%B6lkerfreundschaft%22,_erste_Fahrt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="596" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-71706-0032%2C_MS_%22V%C3%B6lkerfreundschaft%22%2C_erste_Fahrt.jpg/180px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-71706-0032%2C_MS_%22V%C3%B6lkerfreundschaft%22%2C_erste_Fahrt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-71706-0032%2C_MS_%22V%C3%B6lkerfreundschaft%22%2C_erste_Fahrt.jpg/270px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-71706-0032%2C_MS_%22V%C3%B6lkerfreundschaft%22%2C_erste_Fahrt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-71706-0032%2C_MS_%22V%C3%B6lkerfreundschaft%22%2C_erste_Fahrt.jpg/360px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-71706-0032%2C_MS_%22V%C3%B6lkerfreundschaft%22%2C_erste_Fahrt.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-71706-0032,_MS_%22V%C3%B6lkerfreundschaft%22,_erste_Fahrt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Α road traffic policeman of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cities_Police" title="Cities Police">Cities Police</a> in 1960, Athens</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The peak of development was registered principally in manufacture, mainly in the textile and chemical industry and in the sector of metallurgy, the growth rate of which tended to reach 11% during 1965-70. The other large branch where obvious economic and social consequences were brought about, was that of construction. Consideration, a Greek invention, favoured the creation of a class of small-medium contractors on one hand and settled the housing system and property status on the other.</p>
<p>During that decade, youth came forth in society as a distinct social power with autonomous presence (creation of a new culture in music, fashion etc.) and displaying dynamism in the assertion of their social rights. The independence granted to Cyprus, which was mined from the very beginning, constituted the main focus of young activist mobilizations, along with struggles aiming at reforms in education, which were provisionally realized to a certain extent through the educational reform of 1964. The country reckoned on and was influenced by Europe – usually behind time – and by the current trends like never before. Thus, in a sense, the imposition of the military junta conflicted with the social and cultural occurrences.</p>
<p>The country descended into a prolonged political crisis, and elections were scheduled for late April 1967. On 21 April 1967 however, a group of right-wing colonels led by Colonel <a href="/wiki/Georgios_Papadopoulos" title="Georgios Papadopoulos">George Papadopoulos</a> seized power in a <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup d'état</a> establishing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_military_junta_of_1967%E2%80%931974" title="Greek military junta of 1967–1974">Regime of the Colonels</a>. Civil liberties were suppressed, special military courts were established, and political parties were dissolved. Several thousand suspected communists and political opponents were imprisoned or exiled to remote Greek islands. Alleged US support for the junta is claimed to be the cause of rising <a href="/wiki/Anti-Americanism" title="Anti-Americanism">anti-Americanism</a> in Greece during and following the junta's harsh rule.</p>
<p>However, the junta's early years also saw a marked upturn in the economy, with increased foreign investment and large-scale infrastructure works. The junta was widely condemned abroad, but inside the country, discontent began to increase only after 1970, when the economy slowed down. Even the armed forces, the regime's foundation, were not immune: In May 1973, a planned coup by the <a href="/wiki/Hellenic_Navy" title="Hellenic Navy">Hellenic Navy</a> was narrowly suppressed, but led to the mutiny of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HNS_Velos" title="HNS Velos">HNS <i>Velos</i></a>, whose officers sought political asylum in Italy. In response, junta leader Papadopoulos attempted to steer the regime towards a <a href="/wiki/Metapolitefsi" title="Metapolitefsi">controlled democratization</a>, abolishing the monarchy and declaring himself President of the Republic.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Greek_Monarchical_Constitutions">The Greek Monarchical Constitutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: The Greek Monarchical Constitutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>The first constitution of the Kingdom of Greece was the <b><a href="/wiki/Greek_Constitution_of_1844" title="Greek Constitution of 1844">Greek Constitution of 1844</a></b>. On 3 September 1843, the military garrison of Athens, with the help of citizens, <a href="/wiki/3_September_1843_Revolution" title="3 September 1843 Revolution">rebelled</a> and demanded from <a href="/wiki/Otto_of_Greece" title="Otto of Greece">King Otto</a> the concession of a Constitution.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%CE%A0%CE%B1%CE%BB%CE%B9%CE%AC_%CE%92%CE%BF%CF%85%CE%BB%CE%AE_%CF%84%CF%89%CE%BD_%CE%95%CE%BB%CE%BB%CE%AE%CE%BD%CF%89%CE%BD_9737.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3464" data-file-width="4632" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/%CE%A0%CE%B1%CE%BB%CE%B9%CE%AC_%CE%92%CE%BF%CF%85%CE%BB%CE%AE_%CF%84%CF%89%CE%BD_%CE%95%CE%BB%CE%BB%CE%AE%CE%BD%CF%89%CE%BD_9737.jpg/200px-%CE%A0%CE%B1%CE%BB%CE%B9%CE%AC_%CE%92%CE%BF%CF%85%CE%BB%CE%AE_%CF%84%CF%89%CE%BD_%CE%95%CE%BB%CE%BB%CE%AE%CE%BD%CF%89%CE%BD_9737.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/%CE%A0%CE%B1%CE%BB%CE%B9%CE%AC_%CE%92%CE%BF%CF%85%CE%BB%CE%AE_%CF%84%CF%89%CE%BD_%CE%95%CE%BB%CE%BB%CE%AE%CE%BD%CF%89%CE%BD_9737.jpg/300px-%CE%A0%CE%B1%CE%BB%CE%B9%CE%AC_%CE%92%CE%BF%CF%85%CE%BB%CE%AE_%CF%84%CF%89%CE%BD_%CE%95%CE%BB%CE%BB%CE%AE%CE%BD%CF%89%CE%BD_9737.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/%CE%A0%CE%B1%CE%BB%CE%B9%CE%AC_%CE%92%CE%BF%CF%85%CE%BB%CE%AE_%CF%84%CF%89%CE%BD_%CE%95%CE%BB%CE%BB%CE%AE%CE%BD%CF%89%CE%BD_9737.jpg/400px-%CE%A0%CE%B1%CE%BB%CE%B9%CE%AC_%CE%92%CE%BF%CF%85%CE%BB%CE%AE_%CF%84%CF%89%CE%BD_%CE%95%CE%BB%CE%BB%CE%AE%CE%BD%CF%89%CE%BD_9737.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%CE%A0%CE%B1%CE%BB%CE%B9%CE%AC_%CE%92%CE%BF%CF%85%CE%BB%CE%AE_%CF%84%CF%89%CE%BD_%CE%95%CE%BB%CE%BB%CE%AE%CE%BD%CF%89%CE%BD_9737.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Old_Parliament_House,_Athens" title="Old Parliament House, Athens">Old Parliament House, Athens</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Constitution that was proclaimed in March 1844 came from the workings of the "Third of September National Assembly of the Hellenes in Athens" and was a Constitutional Pact, in other words a contract between the monarch and the Nation. This Constitution re-established the Constitutional Monarchy and was based on the French Constitution of 1830 and the Belgian Constitution of 1831.</p>
<p>Its main provisions were the following: It established the principle of monarchical sovereignty, as the monarch was the decisive power of the State; the legislative power was to be exercised by the King – who also had the right to ratify the laws – by the Parliament, and by the Senate. The members of the Parliament could be no less than 80 and they were elected for a three-year term by universal suffrage. The senators were appointed for life by the King and their number was set at 27, although that number could increase should the need arise and per the monarch's will, but it could not exceed half the number of the members of Parliament.</p>
<p>The ministers' responsibility for the King's actions is established, who also appoints and removes them. Justice stems from the King and is dispensed in his name by the judges he himself appoints.</p>
<p>Lastly, this Assembly voted the electoral law of 18 March 1844, which was the first European law to provide, in essence, for <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">universal male suffrage</a>.</p>
<p>The Second National Assembly of the Hellenes took place in <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> (1863–1864) and dealt both with the election of a new sovereign as well as with the drafting of a new Constitution, thereby implementing the transition from <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> to a <a href="/wiki/Crowned_republic" title="Crowned republic">Crowned republic</a>.</p>
<p>Following the refusal of <a href="/wiki/Alfred,_Duke_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" title="Alfred, Duke of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">Prince Alfred of Great Britain</a> (who was elected by an overwhelming majority in the first referendum of the country in November 1862) to accept the crown of the Greek kingdom, the government offered the crown to the Danish prince <a href="/wiki/George_I_of_Greece" title="George I of Greece">George Christian Willem</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_of_Schleswig-Holstein-Sonderburg-Gluecksburg" title="House of Schleswig-Holstein-Sonderburg-Gluecksburg">House of Schleswig-Holstein-Sonderburg-Gluecksburg</a>, who was crowned constitutional King of Greece under the name "George I, King of the Hellenes".</p>
<p>The <b><a href="/wiki/Greek_Constitution_of_1864" title="Greek Constitution of 1864">Constitution of 1864</a></b> was drafted following the models of the Constitutions of <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> of 1831 and of <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> of 1849, and established in clear terms the principle of popular sovereignty, since the only legislative body with reversionary powers was now the Parliament. Furthermore, article 31 reiterated that all the powers stemmed from the Nation and were to be exercised as provided by the Constitution, while article 44 established the principle of accountability, taking into consideration that the King only possessed the powers that were bestowed on him by the Constitution and by the laws applying the same.</p>
<p>The Assembly chose the system of a single chamber Parliament (Vouli) with a four-year term, and hence abolished the Senate, which many accused for being a tool in the hands of the monarchy. Direct, secret and universal elections was adopted as the manner to elect the MPs, while elections were to be held simultaneously throughout the entire nation.</p>
<p>In addition, article 71 introduced a conflict between being an MP and a salaried public employee or mayor at the same time, but not with serving as an army officer.</p>
<p>The Constitution reiterated various clauses found in the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Constitution_of_1844" title="Greek Constitution of 1844">Constitution of 1844</a>, such as that the King appoints and dismisses the ministers and that the latter are responsible for the person of the monarch, but it also allowed for the Parliament to establish "examination committees". Moreover, the King preserved the right to convoke the Parliament in ordinary as well as in extraordinary sessions, and to dissolve it at his discretion, provided, however, that the dissolution decree was also countersigned by the Cabinet.</p>
<p>The Constitution repeated verbatim the clause of article 24 of the Constitution of 1844, according to which "The King appoints and removes his Ministers". This phrase insinuated that the ministers were practically subordinate to the monarch, and thereby answered not only to the Parliament but to him as well. Moreover, nowhere was it stated in the Constitution that the King was obliged to appoint the Cabinet in conformity with the will of the majority in Parliament. This was, however, the interpretation that the modernizing political forces of the land upheld, invoking the principle of popular sovereignty and the spirit of the Parliamentary regime.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek_Parliament,_Chamber_of_Senate.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3026" data-file-width="4539" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Greek_Parliament%2C_Chamber_of_Senate.jpg/220px-Greek_Parliament%2C_Chamber_of_Senate.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Greek_Parliament%2C_Chamber_of_Senate.jpg/330px-Greek_Parliament%2C_Chamber_of_Senate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Greek_Parliament%2C_Chamber_of_Senate.jpg/440px-Greek_Parliament%2C_Chamber_of_Senate.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greek_Parliament,_Chamber_of_Senate.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Chamber of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Senate" title="Greek Senate">Senate</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Royal_Palace" title="Old Royal Palace">Old Royal Palace</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greece_Parliament.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2657" data-file-width="5112" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Greece_Parliament.jpg/220px-Greece_Parliament.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Greece_Parliament.jpg/330px-Greece_Parliament.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Greece_Parliament.jpg/440px-Greece_Parliament.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greece_Parliament.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Old_Royal_Palace" title="Old Royal Palace">Old Royal Palace</a> in Athens hosts the <a href="/wiki/Hellenic_Parliament" title="Hellenic Parliament">Hellenic Parliament</a> since 1929.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>They finally succeeded in imposing it through the principle of "manifest confidence" of the Parliament, which was expressed in 1875 by <a href="/wiki/Charilaos_Trikoupis" title="Charilaos Trikoupis">Charilaos Trikoupis</a> and which, that same year, in his Crown Speech, King George I expressly pledged to uphold: "I demand as a prerequisite, of all that I call beside me to assist me in governing the country, to possess the manifest confidence and trust of the majority of the Nation's representatives. Furthermore, I accept this approval to stem from the Parliament, as without it the harmonious functioning of the polity would be impossible".</p>
<p>The establishment of the principle of "manifest confidence" towards the end of the first decade of the crowned democracy, contributed towards the disappearance of a constitutional practice which, in many ways, reiterated the negative experiences of the period of the reign of <a href="/wiki/Otto_of_Greece" title="Otto of Greece">King Otto</a>. Indeed, from 1864 through 1875 numerous elections of dubious validity had taken place, while, additionally and most importantly, there was an active involvement of the Throne in political affairs through the appointment of governments enjoying a minority in Parliament, or through the forced resignation of majority governments, when their political views clashed with those of the crown.</p>
<p>The <b><a href="/wiki/Greek_Constitution_of_1911" title="Greek Constitution of 1911">Greek Constitution of 1911</a></b> was a major step forward in the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_history_of_Greece" title="Constitutional history of Greece">constitutional history of Greece</a>. Following the rise to power of <a href="/wiki/Eleftherios_Venizelos" title="Eleftherios Venizelos">Eleftherios Venizelos</a> after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goudi_revolt" title="Goudi revolt">Goudi revolt</a> in 1909, Venizelos set about attempting to reform the state. The main outcome of this was a major revision to the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Constitution_of_1864" title="Greek Constitution of 1864">Greek Constitution of 1864</a>.</p>
<p>The most noteworthy amendments to the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Constitution_of_1864" title="Greek Constitution of 1864">Constitution of 1864</a> concerning the protection of human rights, were the more effective protection of personal security, equality in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tax_burden" title="Tax burden">tax burdens</a>, of the right to assemble and of the inviolability of the domicile. Furthermore, the Constitution facilitated expropriation to allocate property to <a href="/wiki/Land_reform" title="Land reform">landless farmers</a>, while simultaneously judicially protecting property rights.</p>
<p>Other important changes included the institution of an Electoral Court for the settlement of election disputes stemming from the parliamentary elections, the addition of new conflicts for MPs, the re-establishment of the State Council as the highest administrative court (which, however, was constituted and operated only under the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Greek_Constitution_of_1927&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Greek Constitution of 1927 (page does not exist)">Constitution of 1927</a>), the improvement of the protection of judicial independence and the establishment of the non-removability of public employees. Finally, for the first time, the Constitution provided for mandatory and free education for all, and declared <a href="/wiki/Katharevousa" title="Katharevousa">Katharevousa</a> (i.e. archaising "purified" Greek) as the <i>"official language of the Nation"</i>.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Greece entered its period of new-won independence in a somewhat different state than Serbia, which shared many of the post-independence economic problems such as land reform. In 1833, the Greeks took control of a countryside devastated by war, depopulated in places and hampered by primitive agriculture and marginal soils. Just as in Serbia, communications were bad, presenting obstacles for any wider foreign commerce. Even by the late 19th century Agricultural development had not advanced as significantly as had been intended as William Moffet, the US Consul in Athens explained:</p>
<p><i>"agriculture is here in the most undeveloped condition. Even in the immediate neighborhood of Athens it is common to find the wooden plow and the rude mattock which were in use 2,000 years ago. Fields are plowed up or scratched over, and crops replanted season after season, until the exhausted soil will bear no more. Fertilizers are not used to any appreciable extent, and the farm implements are of the very rudest description. Irrigation is in use in some districts, and, as far as I can ascertain, the methods in use can be readily learned by a study of the practices of the ancient Egyptians. Greece has olives and grapes in abundance, and of quality not excelled; but Greek olive oil and Greek wine will not bear transportation."</i></p>
<p>Greece had a substantial wealthy commercial class of rural notables and island shipowners, and access to 9,000,000 acres (36,000 km<sup>2</sup>) of land expropriated from Muslim owners who had been driven off during the War of Independence.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_reform">Land reform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Land reform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Construction_of_the_Athens-Piraeus_highway,_1835-36.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1241" data-file-width="1574" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Construction_of_the_Athens-Piraeus_highway%2C_1835-36.jpg/240px-Construction_of_the_Athens-Piraeus_highway%2C_1835-36.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Construction_of_the_Athens-Piraeus_highway%2C_1835-36.jpg/360px-Construction_of_the_Athens-Piraeus_highway%2C_1835-36.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Construction_of_the_Athens-Piraeus_highway%2C_1835-36.jpg/480px-Construction_of_the_Athens-Piraeus_highway%2C_1835-36.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Construction_of_the_Athens-Piraeus_highway,_1835-36.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Construction of the Athens-Piraeus highway by Army engineers, 1835-36</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Land reform represented the first real test for the new Greek kingdom. The new Greek government deliberately adopted land reforms intended to create a class of free peasants. The <i>"Law for the Dotation of Greek Families"</i> of 1835 extended 2,000 drachmas credit to every family, to be used to buy a 12-acre (49,000 m<sup>2</sup>) farm at auction under a low-cost loan plan. The country was full of displaced refugees and empty Ottoman estates.</p>
<p>By a series of land reforms over several decades, the government distributed this confiscated land among veterans and the poor, so that by 1870 most Greek peasant families owned about 20 acres (81,000 m<sup>2</sup>). These farms were too small for prosperity but the land reform signaled the goal of a society in which Greeks were equals and could support themselves, instead of working for hire on the estates of the rich. The class basis of rivalry between Greek factions was thereby reduced.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raisin.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1983" data-file-width="3207" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Raisin.JPG/240px-Raisin.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Raisin.JPG/360px-Raisin.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Raisin.JPG/480px-Raisin.JPG 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raisin.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Exportation of raisin; port of <a href="/wiki/Patras" title="Patras">Patras</a>, late 19th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry">Industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The series of wars between 1912 and 1922 provided a catalyst for Greek industry, with a number of industries such as textiles; ammunition and boot-making springing up to supply the military. After the wars most of these industries were converted to civilian uses. Greek refugees from Asia Minor, the most famous of which was <a href="/wiki/Aristotle_Onassis" title="Aristotle Onassis">Aristotle Onassis</a> who hails from Smyrna (modern Izmir) also had a tremendous impact on the evolution of Greek industry and banking. Greeks held 45% of the capital in the Ottoman Empire before 1914,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Issawi_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Issawi-34">[34]</a></sup> and many of the refugees expelled from Turkey had funds and skills which they quickly put to use in Greece.</p>
<p>These refugees from Asia Minor also led to rapid growth of urban areas in Greece, as the vast majority of them settled in urban centers such as Athens and Thessaloniki. The 1920 census reported that 36.3% of Greeks lived in urban or semi-urban areas, while the 1928 census reported that 45.6% of Greeks lived in urban or semi-urban areas. It has been argued by many Greek economists that these refugees kept Greek industry competitive during the 1920s, as the surplus of labor kept real wages very low. Although this thesis makes economic sense, it is sheer speculation as there is no reliable data on wages and prices in Greece during this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freris_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freris-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Greek industry went into decline slightly before the country joined the EC, and this trend continued. Although worker productivity rose significantly in Greece, labor costs increased too fast for the Greek manufacturing industry to remain competitive in Europe. There was also very little modernization in Greek industries due to a lack of financing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lessons_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lessons-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dichotomization_of_the_drachma">Dichotomization of the drachma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Dichotomization of the drachma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Budgetary problems caused the Greek government to begin an interesting economic experiment, the dichotomization of the drachma. Unable to secure any more loans from abroad to finance the war with Turkey, in 1922 Finance Minister <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protopapadakis" title="Protopapadakis">Protopapadakis</a> declared that each drachma was essentially to be cut in half. Half of the value of the drachma would be kept by the owner, and the other half would be surrendered by the government in exchange for a 20-year 6.5% loan. World War II led to these loans not being repaid, but even if the war had not occurred it is doubtful that the Greek government would have been able to repay such enormous debts to its own populace. This strategy led to large revenues for the Greek state, and inflation effects were minimal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freris_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Freris-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>This strategy was repeated again in 1926 due to the government’s inability to pay back loans incurred from the decade of war and the resettlement of the refugees. Deflation occurred after this dichotomization of the drachma, as well as a rise in interest rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freris_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Freris-35">[35]</a></sup> These policies had the effect of causing much of the populace to lose faith in their government, and investment decreased as people began to stop holding their assets in cash which had become unstable, and began holding real goods.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Great_Depression">The Great Depression</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: The Great Depression">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>As the reverberations of the Great Depression hit Greece in 1932. The Bank of Greece tried to adopt deflationary policies to stave off the crises that were going on in other countries, but these largely failed. For a brief period the drachma was pegged to the US dollar, but this was unsustainable given the country’s large trade deficit, and the only long-term effects of this were Greece’s foreign exchange reserves being almost totally wiped out in 1932. Remittances from abroad declined sharply and the value of the drachma began to plummet from 77 drachmas to the dollar in March 1931 to 111 drachmas to the dollar in April, 1931.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freris_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-Freris-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>This was especially harmful to Greece as the country relied on imports from the UK, France and the Middle East for many necessities. Greece went off the gold standard in April, 1932 and declared a moratorium on all interest payments. The country also adopted protectionist policies such as import quotas, which a number of European countries did during the time period. Protectionist policies coupled with a weak drachma, stifling imports, allowed Greek industry to expand during the Great Depression. In 1939 Greek Industrial output was 179% that of 1928.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freris_35-4"><a href="#cite_note-Freris-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>These industries were for the most part “built on sand” as one report of the Bank of Greece put it, as without massive protection they would not have been able to survive. Despite the global depression, Greece managed to suffer comparatively little, averaging an average growth rate of 3.5% from 1932-1939. The fascist regime of Yannis Metaxas took over the Greek government in 1936, and economic growth was strong in the years leading up to the Second World War.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Shipping">Shipping</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Shipping">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Greek_shipping" title="Greek shipping">Greek shipping</a>.</div>
<p>One industry in which Greece had major success was the shipping industry. Greece’s geography has made the country a major player in maritime affairs from antiquity, and Greece has a strong modern tradition dating from the treaty of Kuchuk-Kajnardji in 1774, which allowed Greek ships to escape Ottoman domination by registering under the Russian flag. The treaty prompted a number of Greek commercial houses to be set up across the Mediterranean and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>, and after independence, Greece's shipping industry was one of the few bright spots in the modern Greek economy during the 19th century.</p>
<p>After both world wars the Greek shipping industry was hit hard by the decline in world trade, but both times it revived quickly. The Greek government aided the revival of the Greek shipping industry with insurance promises following the Second World War. Tycoons such as <a href="/wiki/Aristotle_Onassis" title="Aristotle Onassis">Aristotle Onassis</a> also aided in strengthening the Greek merchant fleet, and shipping has remained one of the few sectors in which Greece still excels.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Greece" title="Tourism in Greece">Tourism in Greece</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eot-paliouri-1962-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1347" data-file-width="2384" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Eot-paliouri-1962-2.jpg/220px-Eot-paliouri-1962-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Eot-paliouri-1962-2.jpg/330px-Eot-paliouri-1962-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Eot-paliouri-1962-2.jpg/440px-Eot-paliouri-1962-2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eot-paliouri-1962-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xenia_(Hotel)" title="Xenia (Hotel)">Xenia</a> Hotel at Paliouri. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chalkidice" title="Chalkidice">Chalkidice</a>, 1962.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It was during the 60s and 70s that tourism, which now account for 15% of Greece's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GDP" title="GDP">GDP</a>, began to become a major earner of foreign exchange. This was initially opposed by many in the Greek government, as it was seen as a very unstable source of income in the event of any political shocks. It was also opposed by many conservatives and by the Church as bad for the country's morals. Despite concerns, tourism grew significantly in Greece and was encouraged by successive governments as it was a very easy source of badly needed foreign exchange revenues.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Greece" title="Agriculture in Greece">Agriculture in Greece</a>.</div>
<p>The resolution of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Turkish_War_(1919-1922)" title="Greco-Turkish War (1919-1922)">Greco-Turkish War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lausanne" title="Treaty of Lausanne">Treaty of Lausanne</a> led to a population exchange between Greece and Turkey, which also had massive ramifications on the agricultural sector in Greece. The tsifliks were abolished, and Greek refugees from Asia Minor settled on these abandoned and partitioned estates. In 1920 only 4% of land holdings were of sizes more than 24 acres (97,000 m<sup>2</sup>), and only .3% of these were in large estates of more than 123 acres (0.50 km<sup>2</sup>). This pattern of small scale farm ownership has continued to the present day, with the small number of larger farms declining slightly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freris_35-5"><a href="#cite_note-Freris-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-World_War_II">Post-World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Post-World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For more details on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Greek_economic_miracle" title="Greek economic miracle">Greek economic miracle</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Athens_Tower.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2352" data-file-width="1791" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Athens_Tower.jpg/200px-Athens_Tower.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Athens_Tower.jpg/300px-Athens_Tower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Athens_Tower.jpg/400px-Athens_Tower.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Athens_Tower.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athens_Tower" title="Athens Tower">Athens Tower</a>, a symbol of the postwar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Economic_Miracle" title="Greek Economic Miracle">Greek Economic Miracle</a> from 1950 to 1973.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Greece suffered comparatively much more than most Western European countries during the Second World War due to a number of factors. Heavy resistance led to immense German reprisals against civilians. Greece was also dependent on food imports, and a British naval blockade coupled with transfers of agricultural produce to Germany led to famine. It is estimated that the Greek population declined by 7% during the Second World War. Greece experienced <a href="/wiki/Hyperinflation" title="Hyperinflation">hyperinflation</a> during the war. In 1943, prices were 34,864% higher compared to those of 1940; in 1944, prices were 163,910,000,000% higher compared to the 1940 prices. The Greek hyperinflation is the fifth worst in economic history, after Hungary’s following World War II, <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>’s in the late 2000s, <a href="/wiki/Yugoslavia" title="Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a>’s in the middle 1990s, and Germany’s following World War I. This was compounded by the country's disastrous civil war from 1944-1950.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freris_35-6"><a href="#cite_note-Freris-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Greek economy was in an extremely poor state in 1950 (after the end of the Civil War), with its relative position dramatically affected. In that year Greece had a per capita GDP of $1,951, which was well below that of countries like Portugal ($2,132), Poland ($2,480), and even Mexico ($2,085). Greece’s per capita GDP was comparable to that of countries like Bulgaria ($1,651), Japan ($1,873), or Morocco ($1,611).</p>
<p>Over the past 50 years Greece has grown much faster than most of the countries that had comparable per capita GDP’s in 1950, reaching a per capita GDP of $30,603 today. This can be compared to the previously stated countries, $17,900 in Portugal, $12,000 in Poland, $9,600 in Mexico, $8,200 in Bulgaria and $4,200 in Morocco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Greece’s growth averaged 7% between 1950 and 1973, a rate second only to Japan's during the same period. In 1950 Greece was ranked 28th in the world for per capita GDP, while in 1970 it was ranked 20th.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_arts">Visual arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Visual arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_art" title="Modern Greek art">Modern Greek art</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_academic_art_of_the_19th_century" title="Greek academic art of the 19th century">Greek academic art of the 19th century</a></div>
<p>Modern Greek art began to be developed around the time of <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romanticism</a>. Greek artists absorbed many elements from their European colleagues, resulting in the culmination of the distinctive style of Greek Romantic art, inspired by revolutionary ideals as well as the country's geography and history. The most important artistic movement of <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_art" title="Modern Greek art">Greek painting</a> in the 19th century was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academic_realism" title="Academic realism">academic realism</a> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_academic_art_of_the_19th_century" title="Greek academic art of the 19th century">Greek academic art of the 19th century</a>), often called in Greece "the <a href="/wiki/Munich_School" title="Munich School">Munich School</a>" because of the strong influence from the <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_Fine_Arts,_Munich" title="Academy of Fine Arts, Munich">Royal Academy of Fine Arts of Munich</a> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de"><i>Münchner Akademie der Bildenden Künste</i></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pit_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-pit-38">[38]</a></sup> where many Greek artists trained. The Munich School painted the same sort of scenes in the same sort of style as Western European academic painters in several countries, and did generally not attempt to incorporate Byzantine stylistic elements into their work. The creation of <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">romantic art</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> can be explained mainly due to the particular relationships that were created between recently liberated <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> (1830) and <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a> during <a href="/wiki/Otto_of_Greece" title="Otto of Greece">King Otto</a>'s years.</p>
<p>Notable sculptors of the new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Kingdom" title="Greek Kingdom">Greek Kingdom</a> were <a href="/wiki/Leonidas_Drosis" title="Leonidas Drosis">Leonidas Drosis</a> whose major work was the extensive neo-classical architectural ornament at the <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_Athens_(modern)" title="Academy of Athens (modern)">Academy of Athens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lazaros_Sochos" title="Lazaros Sochos">Lazaros Sochos</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Georgios_Vitalis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Georgios Vitalis (page does not exist)">Georgios Vitalis</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dimitrios_Filippotis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dimitrios Filippotis (page does not exist)">Dimitrios Filippotis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ioannis_Kossos" title="Ioannis Kossos">Ioannis Kossos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yannoulis_Chalepas" title="Yannoulis Chalepas">Yannoulis Chalepas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgios_Bonanos" title="Georgios Bonanos">Georgios Bonanos</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lazaros_Fytalis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lazaros Fytalis (page does not exist)">Lazaros Fytalis</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theatre">Theatre</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Theatre">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek_theatre" title="Modern Greek theatre">Modern Greek theatre</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:National_theatre_athens_greece.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a9/National_theatre_athens_greece.jpg/220px-National_theatre_athens_greece.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a9/National_theatre_athens_greece.jpg/330px-National_theatre_athens_greece.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a9/National_theatre_athens_greece.jpg/440px-National_theatre_athens_greece.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:National_theatre_athens_greece.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/National_Theatre_of_Greece" title="National Theatre of Greece">National Theatre of Greece</a>, former <i>Royal Theatre</i>, in Athens.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The modern Greek theatre was born after the <a href="/wiki/Greek_War_of_Independence" title="Greek War of Independence">Greek independence</a>, in the early 19th century, and initially was influenced by the Heptanesean theatre and melodrama, such as the Italian opera. The <a href="/wiki/Nobile_Teatro_di_San_Giacomo_di_Corf%C3%B9" title="Nobile Teatro di San Giacomo di Corfù">Nobile Teatro di San Giacomo di Corfù</a> was the first theatre and <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">opera</a> house of modern Greece and the place where the first Greek opera, <a href="/wiki/Spyridon_Xyndas" title="Spyridon Xyndas">Spyridon Xyndas</a>' <i>The Parliamentary Candidate</i> (based on an exclusively Greek <a href="/wiki/Libretto" title="Libretto">libretto</a>) was performed. During the late 19th and early 20th century, the Athenian theatre scene was dominated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revues" title="Revues">revues</a>, <a href="/wiki/Musical_theatre" title="Musical theatre">musical comedies</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operettas" title="Operettas">operettas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nocturnes" title="Nocturnes">nocturnes</a> and notable playwrights included <a href="/wiki/Spyridon_Samaras" title="Spyridon Samaras">Spyridon Samaras</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dionysios_Lavrangas&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dionysios Lavrangas (page does not exist)">Dionysios Lavrangas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theophrastos_Sakellaridis" title="Theophrastos Sakellaridis">Theophrastos Sakellaridis</a> and others.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Theatre_of_Greece" title="National Theatre of Greece">National Theatre of Greece</a> was founded in 1880. Notable playwrights of the modern Greek theatre include <a href="/wiki/Gregorios_Xenopoulos" title="Gregorios Xenopoulos">Gregorios Xenopoulos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nikos_Kazantzakis" title="Nikos Kazantzakis">Nikos Kazantzakis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pantelis_Horn" title="Pantelis Horn">Pantelis Horn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alekos_Sakellarios" title="Alekos Sakellarios">Alekos Sakellarios</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iakovos_Kambanelis" title="Iakovos Kambanelis">Iakovos Kambanelis</a>, while notable actors include <a href="/wiki/Cybele_Andrianou" title="Cybele Andrianou">Cybele Andrianou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marika_Kotopouli" title="Marika Kotopouli">Marika Kotopouli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aimilios_Veakis" title="Aimilios Veakis">Aimilios Veakis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orestis_Makris" title="Orestis Makris">Orestis Makris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Katina_Paxinou" title="Katina Paxinou">Katina Paxinou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manos_Katrakis" title="Manos Katrakis">Manos Katrakis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dimitris_Horn" title="Dimitris Horn">Dimitris Horn</a>. Significant directors include <a href="/wiki/Dimitris_Rontiris" title="Dimitris Rontiris">Dimitris Rontiris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Minotis" title="Alexis Minotis">Alexis Minotis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karolos_Koun" title="Karolos Koun">Karolos Koun</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cinema">Cinema</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Cinema">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_cinema" title="Greek cinema">Greek cinema</a></div>
<p>Cinema first appeared in Greece in 1896 at the <a href="/wiki/1896_Summer_Olympics" title="1896 Summer Olympics">Summer Olympics</a>, but the first actual cine-theatre was opened in 1907. In 1914 the <i>Asty Films Company</i> was founded and the production of long films begun. <i>Golfo</i> (Γκόλφω), a well known traditional love story, is the first Greek long movie, although there were several minor productions such as newscasts before this. In 1931 <a href="/wiki/Orestis_Laskos" title="Orestis Laskos">Orestis Laskos</a> directed <i>Daphnis and Chloe</i> (<i>Δάφνις και Χλόη</i>), contained the first nude scene in the history of European cinema; it was also the first Greek movie which was played abroad. In 1944 <a href="/wiki/Katina_Paxinou" title="Katina Paxinou">Katina Paxinou</a> was honoured with the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Supporting_Actress" title="Academy Award for Best Supporting Actress">Best Supporting Actress</a> <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Academy Award</a> for <i><a href="/wiki/For_Whom_the_Bell_Tolls_(film)" title="For Whom the Bell Tolls (film)">For Whom the Bell Tolls</a></i>.</p>
<p>The 1950s and early 1960s are considered by many as the Greek Golden age of Cinema. Directors and actors of this era were recognized as important historical figures in Greece and some gained international acclaim: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mihalis_Kakogiannis" title="Mihalis Kakogiannis">Mihalis Kakogiannis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alekos_Sakellarios" title="Alekos Sakellarios">Alekos Sakellarios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melina_Mercouri" title="Melina Mercouri">Melina Mercouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nikos_Tsiforos" title="Nikos Tsiforos">Nikos Tsiforos</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iakovos_Kambanelis" title="Iakovos Kambanelis">Iakovos Kambanelis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Katina_Paxinou" title="Katina Paxinou">Katina Paxinou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nikos_Koundouros" title="Nikos Koundouros">Nikos Koundouros</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ellie_Lambeti" title="Ellie Lambeti">Ellie Lambeti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irene_Papas" title="Irene Papas">Irene Papas</a> etc. More than sixty films per year were made, with the majority having film noir elements.</p>
<p>Notable films were <i>Η κάλπικη λίρα</i> (1955 directed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giorgos_Tzavellas" title="Giorgos Tzavellas">Giorgos Tzavellas</a>), <i>Πικρό Ψωμί</i> (1951, directed by Grigoris Grigoriou), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/O_Drakos" title="O Drakos">O Drakos</a></i> (1956 directed by <a href="/wiki/Nikos_Koundouros" title="Nikos Koundouros">Nikos Koundouros</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/Stella_(1955_film)" title="Stella (1955 film)">Stella</a></i> (1955 directed by Cacoyannis and written by Kampanellis). Cacoyannis also directed <i>Zorba the Greek</i> with Anthony Quinn which received Best Director, Best Adapted Screenplay and Best Film nominations. <a href="/wiki/Finos_Film" title="Finos Film">Finos Film</a> also contributed to this period with movies such as <i>Λατέρνα, Φτώχεια και Φιλότιμο</i>, <i>Η Θεία από το Σικάγο</i>, <i>Το ξύλο βγήκε από τον Παράδεισο</i> and many more.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_exiled_royal_family">The exiled royal family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: The exiled royal family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Greek_Royal_Family" title="Greek Royal Family">Greek Royal Family</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Former_King_Constantine_%26_Queen_Anne_Marie_of_Greece_in_Colour.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="12433" data-file-width="6794" height="329" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Former_King_Constantine_%26_Queen_Anne_Marie_of_Greece_in_Colour.jpg/180px-Former_King_Constantine_%26_Queen_Anne_Marie_of_Greece_in_Colour.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Former_King_Constantine_%26_Queen_Anne_Marie_of_Greece_in_Colour.jpg/270px-Former_King_Constantine_%26_Queen_Anne_Marie_of_Greece_in_Colour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Former_King_Constantine_%26_Queen_Anne_Marie_of_Greece_in_Colour.jpg/360px-Former_King_Constantine_%26_Queen_Anne_Marie_of_Greece_in_Colour.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Former_King_Constantine_%26_Queen_Anne_Marie_of_Greece_in_Colour.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Constantine and his wife with their children, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Princess_Theodora" title="Princess Theodora">Princess Theodora</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Prince_Philippos&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Prince Philippos (page does not exist)">Prince Philippos</a>. Photo by <a href="/wiki/Allan_Warren" title="Allan Warren">Allan Warren</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most members of the former royal family are living abroad; <a href="/wiki/Constantine_II_of_Greece" title="Constantine II of Greece">Constantine II</a> and his wife, <a href="/wiki/Queen_Anne-Marie_of_Greece" title="Queen Anne-Marie of Greece">Anne-Marie</a> and unmarried children resided in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> until 2013 when they returned to Greece to reside permanently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghda20_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-ghda20-39">[39]</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Patrilineality" title="Patrilineality">male-line</a> descendants of King <a href="/wiki/Christian_IX_of_Denmark" title="Christian IX of Denmark">Christian IX of Denmark</a> the members of the dynasty bear the title of <i>Prince or Princess of <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a></i>; this is why they are traditionally referred to as Princes or Princesses of <i>Greece and Denmark</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ghda20_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-ghda20-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_kings_of_Greece">List of kings of Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kingdom_of_Greece&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: List of kings of Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_kings_of_Greece" title="List of kings of Greece">List of kings of Greece</a></div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Otto_of_Greece" title="Otto of Greece">Otto of Greece</a> – 6 February 1833 – 23 October 1862</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/George_I_of_Greece" title="George I of Greece">George I of Greece</a> – 30 March 1863 – 18 March 1913</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Constantine_I_of_Greece" title="Constantine I of Greece">Constantine I of Greece</a> – 18 March 1913 – 11 June 1917 and 19 December 1920 – 27 September 1922</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alexander_of_Greece" title="Alexander of Greece">Alexander of Greece</a> – 11 June 1917 – 25 October 1920</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Greece" title="George II of Greece">George II of Greece</a> – 27 September 1922 – 25 March 1924 and 3 November 1935 – 1 April 1947</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Greece" title="Paul of Greece">Paul of Greece</a> – 1 April 1947 – 6 March 1964</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Constantine_II_of_Greece" title="Constantine II of Greece">Constantine II of Greece</a> – 6 March 1964 – 1 June 1973 <i>(monarchy abolished)</i></li>
</ul>
<p><i>Note: The dates signify reign not life span.</i></p>
